Tales of the Early Days.
No. XVI, (and last)—The Pardon that
never came.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
BY PRICE WARUNG
Port Phillip was not yet separated from
New South Wales—and, consequently, Convict
Pierre La Touche was not yet lost sight of by the
high and benign Powers in Sydney,
Those Powers were never so benign as when
they forgot a convict. When they let him slip
out of their ordinardy retentive memories, which
gripped everything about him except his virtues,
they did him the kindest of kindnesses. Oblivion
sometimes brings with it happiness.
But the System declined to be oblivious of
Pierre La Touche’s existence. It assigned him to
Mr. Landon Hughes, of Naluka Tallock, in the
western district of Port Phillip—Fire-eating
Foster Fyans was District Commissioner—and it
was so solicitous respecting him that it required
from botli Mr. Hughes and Captain Fyans an
annual certificate that Convict La Touche was
still in the flesh and handcuffable. The Powers
would not have been satisfied by the simple
assurance that La Touche was not dead. They
would not have slept o’ nights were they not
convinced also that he was conveniently situated
with respect to a suit of fetters and a pair of
manacles.
The truth was that, in the interests of the
Throne, tiro Colonial Powers were themselves
under command to specially watch over the safety
of convict La Touche. It is conceivable they
might have forgotten him and lost sight of him in
tile same Way that a thousand other transports
had passed from their ken had not the exalted
beings who held the keys of Empire dignified La
Touche with a sp cia! minute. “He is not to be
kept in close confinement,” minuted, the Secretary
of State, y but is to bo assigned to some settler in
the interior of superior station, who should, how-
ever, be instructed m the occurrence of any cir-
cumstances ” —the Exalted Being is alone respon-
sible for this elegant preciseness of phrase—“ of a
suspicious and treasonable nature, to bring the
prisoner instantly before the nearest Court, who
shall thereupon take such action as it may consider
necessary.” So the System had to watch him.
And the gods pleased that just as convict La
Touche was becoming a thorn in the side of the
Sydney authorities—inasmuch as for a whole
month’s space they couldn’t determine to whom
he should be assigned—Mr. Landon Hughes, who
had lately taken up land in Western Port Phillip,
should visit Sydney. And Mr. Landon Hughes,
being in want of a “ special ” or educated convict
to serve as tutor and book-keeper, and being him-
self of “superior station” —it was confidentially
stated by the Colonial Secretary that he was the
sdrl of a Royal bigamist—at once consented to
accept La Touche as an assigned man. The
assignment system was in abeyance. But
then over an Act of Council was, of course, the
instruction of the Secretary of State. ’Twas
neither the first time nor the last that a Minister’s
note had set aside a Local Act, for that was the
sort of thing to be expected in the epoch of the
Early Days. The notion was supremely ridiculous
that a Secretary of State for the Colonies had
time to spare for acquainting himself with the.
( details of administration. If he did that h«
would have had no opportunity for gambling al
White’s, or to pursue his liaison with her grace ;
the Duchess of Fraile-Vertu. “Dear Sir R—
once unofficially wrote a prominent Under-Secre-
tary to a college-chum who happened to be pre-
siding oyer the destinies of our Australian colony
at the time. “ I cannot transmit by this packet
the necessary instructions to deal with the matter
of yours No. 45 of this year, because the chief is
perdu. You know his old flame. . . ? He is
with her at. . And, of course, the able and
experienced Under-Secretary could not venture to
interrupt his Minister’s amorous dalliances by
drawing his attention to so bagatellish a business
as a question of a man’s life. That was the
“matter.” Should the man hang? Why not?
Why disturb the holder of the keys of the State
with such a question when he was intent on
amorous conquests ?
Now, Pierre La Touche, in spite of regula-
tions which prevented him from being assigned,
was assigned—shipped off by the schooner Grunt-
ley and in due time delivered to the agents of
Mr. Landon Hughes at Portland, Western Port
Phillip. As he was a chattel, a receipt was given
for his body to the schooner’s skipper. Receipts
were given constantly for bodies—so far as we
know, for “ souls ” never, save in one instance,
to be told hereafter.
* * *
La Touche was the gentlest soul that ever
breathed. In him all the fine qualities of the old
French seignior bloomed in a rich effloresence.
Every woman was to him a Madonna, every child
an expression of supernal innocence. Tall, slight,
refined, broad-browed, with a contour of the face
sharpening to a point that indicated not so much
weakness as a strengtli in repose that would
require an emotional earthquake to disturb it, he
carried in every line of his features, in the calm
penetration of his hazel eye, in his unstudied,
thoughtless freedom of movement, proof that he
was no degenerate scion of a once-powerful race.
It was a marvel to those who knew him in Canada
how he could have sprung immediately from the
superior peasantry. And yet (so the official penal
papers informed the authorities in Sydney) he was
but a peasant of the peasants. Perhaps in some
not distant generation a daughter of one of the
great seigniors of the Beauharnois had wedded a
stalwart son of Acadie, and thus, blending the
yeoman with noble blood, had revived in a later
day the type that might have listened to the song
of Roland as Taileffer chanted it in the ears of
the mailed knights of the Carlovingian epoch.
Clothe La Touche in mail, and no wide stretch of
imagination would have been necessary to have
pictured; him as ringing battle-axe and broad
sword on the cuirass of Saladin. Clothe him in
silken mantle, and doublet, and plumed hat, and
jewelled hose, and he would have been beloved of
ladies of high degree and the song of trou-
badours. Standing on the forty-foot jetty that
the Hentys had run out to berth their
whalers and their produce and stock schooners
from Launceston, he was still the noble, though
his costume was hodden gray and stamped with
the symbol of the slave. Number and prong were
on his garments, and the stained and torn hat
bore a mark that indicated it had once been the
head-gear of a capital offender. His official
description stated him “Farm labourer,” but he
was a gentleman always and everywhere. The
British aristocratic type found numerous repre-
sentatives among the transports, but till Canada,
in her wild burst for freedom in the Thirties,
placed the tools of Papineau and Nelson within
reach of the law of treason, the penal immigration
had included nothing so distinctly gentle as the
Canadians. ‘We cannot find their representatives
to-day. Of the three contingents of Canadian
convicts there are, so far as we can ascertain, no
survivors in Australia, because when amnesty
came seignior and dependent alike pined for their
native provinces, thirsted after the sound of the
rapids, and hungered for the balsamed breath of
the pines by the portages, and returned.
Several stayed, it is true, but freedom had come
to them in another form. Among these latter was
La Touche.
* * *
He invited insult by reason of the very
gentleness that was manifested in his manner and
speech, but he resented it by a calm strength
of disdain that was more irritating to the
affronter than contemptuous retort or emphatic
blow, and it was singular how the lower-class
convicts who had voyaged with him from Sydney
to Portland had, after venting their own vulgarity
upon him, been so conquered by his magnanimity
of self-restraint as to stand between him and
insult from the schooner hands and the jetty-men.
Crossing the _ gang-plank, and carrying his
“duds ” with him, a jetty-hand, one of Henty’s
assigned servants, gave the plank a push with his
foot. La Touche would have fallen, had not
another transport, follovying him in his passage
from the hold, caught him, and by an unusual
exercise of strength saved him from a tumble, and
possibly an immersion in the sea. As soon as the
old man, still quivering with the shock, once
reached the jetty, the fellow who had helped him,
and who had himself led an attack of coarse words
and ruder gestures upon the Canadian at the
beginning of the voyage, turned and struck the
jetty-hand.
“Don’t yer twig a swell w’en you see him.
This’ll larn yer manners, ol’ codger !” And a
fight was only stopped by Frank Henty,* then, as
ever, as brave as he was courteous, thrusting him-
self between the two fellows. Unfortunately,
when La Touche noticed this incident, he
expressed his thanks to Henty with an accuracy
and refinement of phrase that surprised young
Frank, for he took off his cap, forgetting, in the
impulse of the moment, that the man whom he
saluted was a convict.
“Mister Frank, sir, Mr. Frank !” cried one of
the station-overseers who had come down to the
schooner-side to receive the Merino Downs detach-
ment of assigned hands, “Don’t you see he’s a
lag?”
The red blood rushed to La Touche’s face as he
checked himself in the bow with which he was
unconsciously returning young Henty’s salutation.
He substituted for it a touch of his hat and a few
words, “ I thank you,” he said to the overseer,
“ for reminding me of—what I am. But, to you,
sir,’’ turning to Henty, “a myriad thanks for
reminding me of what I was.”
And then he ranged himself with the other
convicts the schooner had disembarked, who were
waiting to be claimed with the rest of her cargo.
* * *
This was the manner of the arrival of La
Touche in Portland—his departure from it took
place the day after. One of Mr. Landon Hughes’
bullock-drays was in Portland for stores, and, of
course, that commodity and chattel, La Touche,
was fastened up with other goods by the grazier’s
agent. Literally fastened, we say, inasmuch
as the bull-puncher being compelled in due rou-
tine to give a receipt to the agent for La Touche’s
body, declined to be responsible for a transport’s
safety unless he was secured to the waggon.
“No bolter for me !” he said, “ an’ I’m
told as this cove’s a swell. I ain’t no faith in
swells !” And the agent, thinking it was only a
chattel and not an atom of refined humanity with
which he had to deal, allowed the brutal, tur-
bulent fellow to have his way. A superannuated
bullock-whip tied convict La Touche, the descen-
dant of nobles, to the dray. The “fall,” a single
strip of hide, was wound round his waist; a
length of yarn fastened his hands behind him ; the
belly” of the whip was gathered round a staple,
and so, twelve feet behind the dray, convict La
louche went out of Portland. As the bullocky
was turning the track leading to what is now the
lort Fairy-road, La Touche turned to look at the
sea. The whole of his high-strung nature went
out in tnat last lingering glance. He did not
know it was his last. That was perhaps the
blessedness of it; or, perhaps, that was the horror
of it.
Frank Henty, striding after the dray with an
undefined desire in his heart to help the old man,
somehow caught the glance. He had gone up
half thinking that he would present the convict
with a dollar or two, or, what was the same thing,
“ orders on Henty’s.” But when he reached the
dray, he dared not offer the paper to La Touche.
He had seen his face. To the bullocky, however,
he was less delicately reticent, and, in exchange
for Henty’s orders, he bought the old man’s release
from the galling thraldom to the dray. Nor did
the old man suspect why the knot was so soon
undone.
* * *
The homestead of Langdon Hughes was
40 miles distant from Portland, and was situated
on the Hopkins. It nestled at the foot of low
hills, and faced a sinuous reach of the river, from
a clearing, where once a forest of acacia fringed
the banks by the ford. Across the ford was the
main approach to the house. On the third day
after leaving Portland, the bullock-puncher, who
had been a Torquemada in miniature to the un-
fortunate patriot of the Canadas, hauled up his
team on the farther bank from the house. A
little girl, still on the line which margins infancy
and almost imperceptibly demarcates it from
youth, ran down to the bank on the opposite side
of the stream. The cracking of the monster whip,
and the adjectival eloquence of the bullocky, had
aroused the attention of the household, and the
quick ears of the child being the first to distin-
guish the sounds which heralded the approaching
dray over the noise of the rumbling current of the
river, she was the first to welcome La Touche.
Not, of course, that the System provided for the
welcoming of convicts on reaching the homes of
their assigned masters—not at all. But children
those buds of Paradise blown by zephyrs to
bless the earth—have not invariably a respect for
rule and a regard for regulation. Little Alice
Hughes was doing a distinctly improper thing in
rushing eagerly across the uneven stones which
formed the foot-way of the ford, and holding out
one tiny hand, while tendering him with the other
a small bunch of wild flowers, “Youth’ teacher
isn’th you?” she lisped.
Vv hat were La Touche’s thoughts before she had
run up to him, no one can say, but certainly his
treatment from Landon Hughes himself in Syd-
ney, and from Landon Hughes’ agent in Port-
land, had not prepared him for so felicitous a
reception. The childish accents touched the well-

springs of emotion which were intangible to the
assaults of indignity, and, while he took her hands
with a gesture of grace, he found no word to
utter.
The child would have had him go straight to
the house, and unfamiliar as yet with those little
eccentricities of the System which placed him—a
prisoner of State—under the control of an illite-
rate and brutal “bullocky,” he would have fol-
lowed her, had not the artist in blasphemy and
beast-punching sworn coarsely at him to hold his
steps.
“ I ain’t got no receipt for ye yet, my swell
lag, an’ yer ain’t a-goin’ out o’ my sight till I
gets one. Th’ * cove ’ ken do wot he likes wid yer
then—he ken take yer in the front door and put
yer in th’ drorin’-room an’ he choose —but wi’ me
yer go till I gets a receipt for yer body ! ”
“Come on—come ’long!” said the child, “I’ll
rayth you over ! ” She turned and invited him
by her winsomeness of look and movement to
accept her challenge, but he stood as one be-
numbed. This man who had led a company of
patriots, and who was a prisoner of State, and
about whom the Privy Councillors of England were
even then agitating themselves, stood mute—
obedient —paralysed—at the order of the pettiest
of bush tyrants. The child did not understand
it—nor, we may as well confess, can we—but,
then, there are lots of things about the System not
perfectly understandable now-a-days.
“Come’long, come ’long,” repeated the child.
“ You’th to come to the ’chool-room. Prith’ner
Jim—dith you know prith’ner Jim ? ” Then she
looked in his face, and he had to find words for an
answer. “No,” he said. “No —who was he?”
“Prith’ner Jim —Oh ’e teached me, and, oh,
wath’nt it funny ? We lotht Prith’ner Jim—he
ran away to the black people, cauth Pa whipped
him. You won’t run away, wil’loo, if Pa whipth
’oo?” The inquiry cut him like the stroke of a
lash itself. “No,” he said; “but your Pa will
not whip me ”
“ Oh yeth he will—Pa whipth everybothy ! ”
* * *
The child crossed the ford somewhat dis-
appointed at not having the opportunity of show-
ing to her new preceptor the school-room which
had so charmed Prisoner Jim, for that was the
purport of the communication she had desired to
make to La Touche, but which she had broken off
to wound him with her innocent reference to her
father’s propensity for flogging. The convict,
however, was to know the school-room soon
enough.
For the bullocky introduced him ten minutes
later to the Overseer in charge during Mr. Landon
Hughes’ absence. “ ’Ere’s the new patter slinger,
Mister Trench. Give us your fist for him, sir.”
“ Right, Sam, as soon as I’ve ’dentified him.
Go into that room there, you chap—that long
lean-to by the sheoaks—and strip.”
“Strip, sir ? ”
“ Yes, a-’course ! I’ve heard of you.”
The overseer’s voice softened from its official
harshness. “Mr. Hughes wrote by last schooner
he’d been given a state prisoner, an’ special care
was to be taken.”
“ But, pardon me - why should I strip? These
clothes are all I have ! ” Under caution, he spoke
—as though he suffered inwardly.
“Well”— the steadfast look of the convict
confused the overseer who had been accustomed to
“assignments” of a different brand. “I must
know your marks—‘every pimple of you’; the
master says.”
“ I shall not run away ! ”
“ The chap before you did.”
“ I give you my parole—my word ! ”
A convict’s word ! The overseer, originally one
of Henty’s hands, imported to Launceston from a
Kentish farm-steading, smiled derisively—for a
moment. Then he rose superior to the station
traditions, and said: “I’ll trust you, Go into
that room—into the school-room, but you needn’t
strip.”
The Overseer looked after the tall figure as it
stepped towards the lean-to; was then surprised
—and yet not surprised when he came to think
over the matter later—to notice the figure pausing,
revolving on its heel and returning.
“One word, Monsieur.”
“That’s French, isn’t it? Mr. Hughes says
you are not to be allowed to speak in French ?”
La Touche smiled. “Eh! I beg pardon—l
did not know. But I will withdraw my parole—
my word—if .”
“ Why ? Do you mean as you will escape ?”
“Yes—if, sir, I am beaten —whipped, I will
run away then if I can !”
“If we flog you? Oh, I see! Well, you’re a
straight cove, anyhow. An’ if you’re always as
straight”—the figure drew itself up—“l don’t
mean as you won’t be—you oughtn’t to fear a
flogging here !”
“I never lie —therefore I tell you what I shall
do. I tell you now because I know you are good
and kind to me as you do not insist upon stri—
upon the searching of me. I shall not be whipped
—no one will whip me.” He paused to correct
his grammar. In his excitement he slipped into
unconsciousness of our idiom. “No one shall whip
me—and live ! I will murder who whips me, and
then I will go away to the black people. I have
led Indians —I will lead them !”
Theoverseer’s first impulse was to laugh outright,
the notion of leading blacks to battle was so ludi-
crous. The earnestness of the transport’s manner,
however, was so apparent, and the intense bitter-
ness of his hate for the degrading lash so vibrant
in his speech, that he restrained his merriment.
The past of the convict, a survival of the Middle
Ages, confronted the raw colonist in this patch of
newly-settled country, and impressed him.
“Well,” he said, “ there’s no beating about the
bush with you. And as far as I’m concerned, you
need never fear being flogged. As to the master,
I can’t say. You see, he’s a high and mighty sort,
is Mr. Hughes—rules everything, even the missus
and me, with an iron hand when he’s here, an’—
p’r’aps I oughtn’t to say it, but you’re different
tram the others we’ve had an’ I can trust you—he
believes in the cat for everything.”
“ That was what the little girl said.”
“ Oh, ’ave you seen missie? An’ she said so?”
“ She said Mr. Hughes ”
“Call him ‘Master!’ That’s a nile o’ the
station ! He’d flog you for not saying it!”
“I will call no man master!” replied the
Canadian, foolish and short-sighted in his pride.
“ Now ! ”
“Then, God help you, if he speaks to you when
he comes. “ It’ll be master or the cat.”
“It will be the cat then.” And bowing, not
thinking that he owed something to the magnani-
mity of the Overseer for overlooking the indirect
disregard of the System’s regulations shown by
his substitution of a bow for a salute, he went to
the school-room.
* * »
Roughly and hastily constructed bark
walls, unceiled, with gaping floor-planks, for the
locally-cut timber was unseasoned, an attempt
had yet been made to impart to the interior a
Woi.kk’s Schnapps may be relied upon in all
seasons as the purest and best of liquors.
pleasing aspect. A map of the world, a few crude
drawings initialled “J.”—probably the work of
“Prith’ner Jim” —and a pair of small globes
formed the educative appliances, but some one,
most likely the child’s mother, had, with natural
taste, arranged specimens of foliage and some
blacks’ weapons, so as to relieve the bareness of
the department. Curiously contrasting, however,
with the softening influences of the decorations
were the heavy bolt and padlock on the door.
Instinctively Transport La Touche knew the
school-room was his living-room as well. And
his prison.
His prison, but before long his home. Mrs.
Hughes was one of those delicately nurtured
women who, the very salt of the earth, seemed to
waste existence on the dreariness of the bush.
And the child took after her and not after the
father.
Between them, they made his life easy and
bright till the husband and father came home.
Mr. Hughes was detained longer than he expected
by the gaieties of Sydney. Colonel Barney’s
amateur theatrical troupe was in splendid form—
and his tutor and book-keeper had almost for-
gotten the risks contingent on “master’s” arrival.
The assigned hands took their cue from the mis-
tress and overseer Trench, and the latter had
not seen reason to alter the treatment with
which he had received La Touche. So the
transport felt that his lines had fallen in pleasant
places, and had sent an oral message to that
effect to Frank Henty, who had enquired from
the bullockies respecting him. The System, he
thought, improved upon acquaintance.
* * #
However, the son of a Very Great Person-
age returned to his principality, three months after
La Touche, and the bush, which was breathing
healing music to La Touche’s wounded nature,
became sudden discord.
“Fa’ver,” said his little daughter to Hughes
one day shortly after his return, “you haven’t
been to the schoolroom. Teaoherth drawingth
isth so p’etty.” La Touche had sketched for the
child with coloured crayons first some native
flowers, and then her portrait, and the girl,
thinking of them, became eager to exhibit them to
her father—who, son of a Very Great Personage
though he was, was not wholly blind to the
winsomeness of childhood. He promised her to
visit the room.
She ran off to tell La Touche of the impending
honor.
Mr. Landon Hughes entered the, lean-to.
Convict La Touche was standing by the table
which served as school-desk, and, as his master
entered, bowed. Mrs. Hughes had always
accepted the bow in lieu of the salute; so, too,
the overseer. Mr. Landon Hughes was, however,
more faithful to the spirit and the letter of the
System, as befitted the dignity of his magistracy.
“S’lute,” he said, “Don’t you know your duty
yet, pris’ner?”
La Touche flushed and obeyed with a careful:
“Pardon, Monsieur.”
“What’s that? I am ‘master’ here —I’m
Monsieur only to gentleman.”
The moment of ordeal had come—but passed ;
the child intervening as with charming haste she
drew her father towards where, over the end wall,
her portrait hung. “Come here, fav’er, come
here, an’ see my picture.”
The father, looking up, saw a sketch of his child
done with no inconsiderable degree of artistic
skill. Standing one night on the verandah of the
homestead, La Touche had seen the child, dressed
for her sleeping cot, at her mother’s knee. He
carried the vision away with him and had trans-
ferred it later to paper with the child herself as
model. Beneath the drawing he had inscribed
the legend, “ Dieu M’ en Garde !”
The father looked at the drawing and was
pleased. He looked at the inscription; and was
displeased.
The abominable treason !—to introduce French
into a magistrate’s homestead —French, the lan-
guage of traitors, of the men who ought to have
been drawn, hanged, and quartered ! He turned
on his heel, and spoke sternly to the assigned
man. “ How dare you, pris’ner, disobey the
special regulation made in your case—you’re pro-
hibited, did you not know, from using the French
language under all circumstances?”
La Touche, paling at the tone of insult, as he
had before flushed, replied with respect, “Sir!”
he said, “ I am not conscious of offence —the
drawing of the child —’tis but appropriate to the
subject—a child’s prayer. And Madame was
pleased .”
“Madame—Sir,” retorted Hughes. “I make
my people say Mistress and Master. Even I make
my free people say that to whom I pay wages, and
I don’t intend to let you be any exception to this
rule. ” La Touche remained silent.
“Why,” went on Hughes, “did you use
French ?”
“ I see that therein I was in fault, sir.”
“Master, man, Master! That is the rule of
this estate.”
La Touche held his tongue.
“ Repeat your answer.”
“ I see, sir .”
“ Master, I say, Master !”
“ I will say ‘ Master ’ to no man !” That
improvement upon acquaintance which the System
had exhibited was not genuine after all.
For convict La Touche was ordered a dozen
then and there. The regulation prohibiting
magistrates from flogging their own hands in
irregularly-constituted courts had been in force
several years. But 800 miles from the seat of
Government there was some excuse for its being
forgotten. Anyhow, Mr. Landon Hughes forgot
it. And ordered the punishment.
# * #
During the colloquy which had so un-
pleasant a termination, the child had stood by
impatient and large-eyed. She did not under-
stand all that passed. But the procession to the
flogging-tree—the bringing-out of the cat which
hung in the stables—the calling from his work in
the home-paddock of another assigned man : these
she understood ; memorised them ; never to forget
them ; to tell us fifty years afterwards.
“ Mamma, mamma !” she ran from the school-
room to her mother’s parlour, crying out that her
father was about “to whip teacher.” Mrs.
Hughes had on former occasions interposed
between her husband and an intended victim un-
availingly. Her ill-success then would have with-
held her from interference now, had she not been
convinced that La Touche was a man of gentle
feelings and instincts. She remembered, too, what
Overseer Trench had repeated to her, and realised
that to flog the convict meant to drive him to
desperation. She went out to the group by the
flogging-tree.
“ Landon,” she said ; “Landon—one moment,
dear !”
He turned, remonstrating petulantly with her
for her presence there. “You will interfere in
these matters,” he said. “Please go inside !"
“ I shall not detain you more than a moment,
dear. Please come.”
He followed her into the house, resolved, once
for all, to take care that neither now nor at any
future time should her objection to the lash con-
trol “justice.”
Once inside, Mrs. Hughes told her husband of
Ha Touche’s threat to the overseer. Hughes
laughed. “ I think I’ll give him another dozen
for that.”
“ Oh, Landon, Landon, can you not see the
man is a gentleman ?”
“Gentleman!” he laughed. “But I will not
have the station rules disobeyed.”
“But, dear, this once ” the child here ran
in, in tears. Too young to grasp the whole
significance of the punishment, she had seen
something of the suffering and the pain, and
“ Teacher ” had won the faithful, unreasoning
love of her tender nature. “ You won’t beat
teacher, fav’er?” she pleaded.
But Hughes brushed wife and child aside, and
went out —perhaps with a view of carrying out his
order, perhaps not.
• * *
Just then the halloo of a horseman came
from the other side of the creek. It was the boy
who had been sent in to Portland for the mail
returning.
With the hunger for news that characterises
every denizen of the bush, Hughes waited till the
boy brought the bag across. What matter if he
extended the humiliation of La Touche? He
must have some idea at first of the nature of his
correspondence.
The first letter he opened was one from the
Colonial Secretary, Sydney, and informed him
that “Home” despatches had been received
which stated that, pending the final decision of
the Privy Council on the question remitted to it
concerning the legality of the sentence under
which the Canadian rebels had been transported
to New South Wales, that provisional amnesty
had been granted to La Touche and his com-
patriots. “But,” went on the despatch, “the
transport named in the margin (that was La
Touche) is not to be permitted to leave your em-
ploy till the Secretary of State’s formal pardon
has been received.”
Once, and twice, and thrice, did Hughes read
this. Perhaps— he was dubious—punishment by
the lash might be risky under the circumstances.
The man was educated—was before the Privy
Council—was a prisoner of State —the order might
come for final release at any time; so, while
cursing the officials that put him to the bother
and expense of bringing this assigned fellow all
the way from Sydney, he still thought it best to
act a merciful part. “Punishment postponed!”
he growled, and went inside to finish the perusal
of his correspondence.
* # *
The postponed penalty was never inflicted.
Nor was “Master” insisted on again. To La
Touche’s surprise, and Mrs. Hughes’ delight,
Hughes, on the contrary, showed the convict
something of external consideration, but of the
amnesty and the possibility of a pardon he said
nothing. Neither did he tell his wife of that
despatch.
He had remitted the penalty, but nevertheless
he punished the transport —consciously and of in-
tent, punished him. He had determined to say
nothing of the pardon.
Many another fine Australian gentleman of the
Early Days acted similarly.
• « #
Crave for liberty as La Touche might,
pray for it as he did, though the restraints upon
him became slighter and more and more silken as
the girl grew up and became fonder of him, the
pardon never came. So Hughes said. And La
Touche, knowing the System was often forgetful,
believed him.
Enfranchisement came, however, in the year of
the gold discovery. Free hand and assigned hand
alike had started for the gold-diggings, till the 25
or 30 men whom the station-work had kept busy
became two —La Touche and one other.
La Touche gave up his teaching and bookkeep-
ing for such rougher work as he was equal to. One
day in December, a hot and broiling day, that
recalled the grim horrors of that awful Thursday
of the previous February, he had ridden towards
the eastern boundary of the run. He did not
return at nightfall, and Hughes at last thought
that he, too, had been smitten by the gold-fever
and had gone to the diggings. Only Mrs. Hughes
and the girl knew better. For two days he was
absent, and then the girl, who knew her way like
a tracker through bush and scrub and over plain
and creek, found him a-babbling, not of green
fields, but of forests and rivers, rapids and por-
tages, of L’Acadie and the seigniorv of Beau-
harnois, and of the fatal attack upon the church
at Odell Town when he was captured. And of
one other thing he babbled ; of the pardon that had
never come—to him.
In the delirium caused by sunstroke, he won-
dered why he had been forgotten. England was
not wont to treat her political prisoners with such
utter forgetfulness—she amnestied those she did
not hang—yet she had forgotten him. And in
the babbling which told the girl of the great desire
of his heart after freedom, he passed away. The
girl wept because he did not recognise her.
* # #
Yet the pardon had come, and with it an
extract from the Privy Council’s decision in his
favour, and the girl found both when, a grown
woman, in 1860, she perused her dead father’s
papers. She knew then when the pardon had
come, and wept again—over her teacher’s loss
and her father’s shame.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tire Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tup Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, hut is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere,
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William MacLeod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
>'Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, nr Newspaper-Clippings. 41l
communications will be regarded 'as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS.
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS,

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 24, 1892,
Insidious Imperialism.
The Australian people, while acclaiming
the idea of National Federation, had the
singular foresight to disclaim at the same
time, by innumerable declarations, Imperial
Federation. They applauded The first steps
as movements towards National Federation ;
they recoil from the steps it is now sought
to precipitate them into, because they begin
to apprehend that the Draft Bill of the Con-
vention embodies Imperial Federation ! The
cloven hoof can be detected readily enough,
once attention is directed to the right place.
Its existence has been concealed by a drapery
of fine phrases of a strongly democratic
pattern. The character of the draft consti-
tution is artfully disguised by its name—
“ The Constitution of the Commonwealth.”
This is a style strongly suggestive of liberty
and even of republicanism. Sir Henry
Parkes habitually delivers himself of sound-
ing sentences carrying a flavour of separatism.
He prates of an Australian nation, taking an
equal place among the nations of the world,
sending its ambassadors to every court, and
hoisting a flag of its own. Not one of these
suggestive sayings but is absolutely and
diametrically belied by the actual provisions
and tendencies of the proposed Constitution.
* # «
The Australian “colonies” are depen-
dencies now. Federated in accordance with
the Convention Bill, their dependent condi-
tion would be confirmed, accentuated, and
even aggravated. The powers allotted to the
Governor-General would be, by virtue of
various clauses of the Bill, collateral with
those which define his authority, such as no
Governor of an Australian province has ever
been vested with. Appointed by the Crown,
ho will be entitled to exercise, subject to the
provisions of this Constitution, “ such powers
and functions as the Queen may think fit to
assign to him.” The suggestion of limita-
tion conveyed in the phrase, “ subject to the
provisions of this Constitution is most
misleading. In reality, the provi ■ us of the
Constitution limit the powers of t no Gover-
nor not at all, save that ho cannot enact
laws. But in executive functions he will be
effectively autocratic. Even in those cases
in which his executive authority is ostensibly
limited by the concurrence of the Executive
Council being required, such apparent check
is altogether delusive. The members of the
Executive Council are to be chosen by the
Governor himself, and to hold office during
his pleasure. They need not be members of
either house of the Federal Parliament.
They need not be Australians. The Gover-
nor may take the officers out of a British
man-of-war just arrived and make them his
Executive Council, He could manufacture
an Executive Council out of the flunkeys
who wait on him at table or stand behind his
carriage. Lest any reader consider that
such a provision is too monstrous to be
credible, we quote the clause of the draft
bill
Chap, ii,, sec. 2; There shall he a Council to aid and
advise the Governor-General in the Government of the
Commonwealth, and such Council shall be styled the
Federal Executive Council; and the persons who are to
be members of the Council shall be from time to time
chosen and summoned by the Governor-General and
sworn as Executive Councillors, and shall hold office
during his pleasure.
• * *
An attempt has been made to huddle out
of sight the obvious Imperialistic intention
of these traitorous provisions by the poor
plea that in pursuance of the established
course of constitutional procedure, no Vice-
roy would attempt to exert the immense
powers so conferred upon him. But the
uusoundness of such extenuation, and either
the insincerity or the dulness of those who
urge it, can be perceived after a moment’s
reflection. The Sovereign of Great Britain
who retains quite as large nominal powers,
never during late reigns has attempted or
would venture to exert them. But the power
of the Sovereign and her technically constitu-
tional prerogatives are effectually held in
check and restrained by the power of the
Commons and people of Britain. That is
to say, there is a real physical strength more
than balancing the influence of the Crown.
Wei’e the Sovereign to insist on using the
full powers with which the Crown is yet
invested, the result would be insurrection
and revolution. There is a real balance and
a real check. But with respect to the
technical powers of the Crown, by its Repre-
sentative, in relation to the people of
Australia, there is no such balance and no
such check.
* * *
The situation which requires to be pro-
vided against is the possible antagonism of
Imperial as against Australian interests.
In such case the Crown would really
have the support of the people of
England, and the Governor - General
would be the exponent and the instrument
of the will of the Crown and the Commons
of Great Britain—in fact, of the whole
might of the Mother Country. Against this
the concentrated repugnance of the Austra-
lian people would probably be as naught.
The Governor-General could, without anxiety
and without straining the written constitu-
tion concocted by the Federation Convention,
exert all the powers with which that Consti-
tution arms him. With an Executive
Council, imported from England, holding all
Ministerial positions and so directing all the
machinery of the Federation, he could over-
ride the will of the Parliament and of the
nation, and govern in accordance with the
laws, but also, as to executive acts, in defer-
ence to such instructions as he might receive
from his English masters. Even the consti-
tutional safeguard of witholding supplies
would be unavailable to the Australians, for
as an industrial people they tax themselves
mainly for their own convenience and
requirements. It would be no embarrass-
ment to an English Governor-General were
the Federal Legislature to refuse moneys to
maintain the public service and the public
works. The very sums which they might
ivish to withhold would be beyond their
reach, because the salaries of the Governor-
General and of his Ministers are provided
for in the draft constitution itself, and would
be, consequently, exempt fromannual votings.
Imperial Federation peeps out from a score
of lurking-places in the Draft Bill of the
Convention, and to give a vote approving of
the main principles of that bill, as the Legis-
lature is now invited to do, would be to seal
with approbation a document which signs
away all the essentials of that national
liberty which it is the duty of every patriotic
Australian to guard.
# * #
There is an affectation now of pretending
to regard the Convention Bill as a sort of
Federation Bible—an inspired work, for
which no substitute could be provided. All
who refuse to receive this precious piece of
imposture as divine are to be branded as
heretics and schismatics. Just as the
Catholic Church denounces as beyond the
pale of Christianity all who refuse to accept
its particular dogmas, so the high priests of
the Convention pretend to excommunicate
as enemies to Federation everyone who
objects to bow the knee before their idol—
the Bill of the Convention. Unless a man
be a Federationist of the Draft Bill
sect, he is an enemy to Federation.
This is sheer impertinence and gross
audacity. It is full time that the mask
of patriotism bo pulled from the faces
of the vain fools or plotting conspirators who
strive to impose subjection upon the people
of Australia under the guise of conferring on
them national status. The clear duty of
true National Australians is to repudiate the
Convention Bill, and to indicate how very
different a measure will be that which they
are willing to accept as a preservative of
Australian liberties, and a key to open the
way to liberties yet more extended.
Government by Contradiction —Again!
The closing year leaves, so far as concerns the
five Parliaments of Australia, much the same
record as many profitless years which have gone
before. It is a record of wasted declamation,
broken hopes, lost aspirations, unkept promises,
riot, recrimination, party struggles as confused
as the battles of the kites and the crows; cease-
less debates about nothing, the weary splitting
of useless straws, the aimless shuffling of
dishonest politicians into and out of office, the
constant grabbing after Government billets and
salaries, the perennial announcement by every
party that the country will be lost if the other
party is in office, and that the country will be
saved if it is in office itself, the perpetual
formation of new cliques, which appear and
vanish again like bubbles on the sea, ipotions for
adjournment, motions of want of confidence —
everything, in fact, except actual business.
There is practically nothing to show as the
work of ten Houses of Parliament, save the
frantic struggles of every faction to harry the
other faction away from the national hog-trough,
where it is the joy and pride of the pig in office to
gorgehimself with his front hoofs in the swill; and
the complaints of the hog at the trough about
therude manner in which he is shoved by the hog
behind. The latest conflict between these two
specimens of gluttony has arisen in New South
Wales, where the Opposition animal has moved,
for the third time, or thereabouts, this year, that
it is his turn to get his forward extremities in
the swill, and as there is now little more than a
week remaining until the barren year comes to
a close, this will probably be the last motion of
the kind during 1892, But though this is
probable, it is by no means certain. Seldom
does a week pass without some politician in
some of the Australias rising to move the old,
dry-as-dust resolution, “that this Government
does not possess the confidence of the country.”
# # •
Cabinet-wrecking, or attempted Cabinet-
wrecking, is an established profession in Aus-
tralia nowadays. It is a branch of politics by
itself, and is generally followed by cranks with
no vestige of constructive ability, no originality,
no administrative capacity, and no other
capacity that is visible to the naked eye,
men who have never drafted one useful measure,
or suggested one solitary reform, or contributed
one idea of the slightest value to the aggregate
wisdom of the nation. Bryan O’Loghlen, the
bald devastator of Victorian politics —the
chaotic Broguey Man whose only inspiration is
that the country should live on “ postherity ”
—is probably the most conspicuous professor of
this profitless art south of the equator. His
no-confidence motions are never, or hardly ever,
carried, but he moves them with the painful
regularity of the old whim-horse that walks in
the same well-worn groove till death inter-
venes. G. H. Reid, of New South Wales,
though a comparatively new exponent of this
branch of the great mystery of uselessness,
probably ranks next to him as a public obstruc-
tion ; and the other provinces have also their
wreckers Of more or less note. It requires no
intelligence to be a Cabinet-wrecker ; all that
is necessary is to rise and state in a wooden
voice, and at frequent intervals, “ that this
Government does not possess the confidence of
the country,” and then there begins an un-
bounded flow of declamation that is like the
letting in of many dirty waters. When the
professional no-confidence man is not on the job
other varieties of obstructionists take his place.
There is the professional individual who nightly
moves the adjournment of the House in order
that he may babble in a solemn monotone about
things in general, from the cholera in Europe
down to the forfeiture of a selection in the
back-blocks ten years previously. There is
the individual who habitually rises to
demand a select committee to inquire
into everything, with power to send for every-
body. There is the individual—loo of him—
who wants to ask a separate question—or 12
separate questions—every night; and the one
who always wants the House counted ; and the
one who perpetually moves that the chairman
should report progress ; and the other one who
“ objects ” to everything and divides the House
on every possible opportunity. And the only
night when these assorted obstructions are not
on hand the wrecker generally rises and booms
out once more his old formula, “ That this
Government does not possess the confidence of
the country.” Whereupon, according to pre-
cedent, all business must cease until all the
time-worn speeches have been delivered over
again, and the same old result has been arrived
at by the same circuitous method once more,
* * *
All these troubles are essential features in the
absurd system of Party Government. In every
Parliament conducted under this stupid and
nefarious system the only object of the Opposi-
tion is to shunt the Cabinet out of office. The
easiest way to effect this purpose is to demon-
strate that the Cabinet is absolutely useless
and powerless, and thereby to reduce the
nation to that state of utter disgust in which
any change seems to be a change for the better ;
therefore, it is the policy of the Opposi-
tion to block every separate item of business
day and night, from the beginning of
the year to the end. Then when the session
closes in utter barrenness it is the business of
the Opposition to stump the country with
declamations about the impotence of the Minis-
try which has promised so much and accom-
plished so very little, and to point to the results
of its own obstructive tactics as proof that the
Government is effete and discredited, and that
the country will presently be lost unless it is
removed from office. The motions for adjourn-
ment, the motions for select committees, the
motions that the House be counted, and that
the Chairman should “report progress,” the
want-of-confidence motions, the questions, the
objections to formal motions, three-fourths of
the debates, the personal explanations, the
amendments, the points of order—nearly all of
them have but one object: to prevent the
Government doing anything in order that it
may become discredited because it failed to do
anything. Even the strongest Australian
Government never effects any valuable reform
nowadays except by long years of patient
effort; a weak Government, or one that is only
moderately strong, never effects anything of
value at all. The only time when the eternal
obstruction breaks down is when some gigantic
swindle—such as the Queensland Land-Grant
Railway Bill —is being put through, and there
is enough plunder to give everybody of conse-
quence a share. An honest measure which has
no plunder attached to it is invariably obstructed
to the uttermost, and the more valuable it is
the more carefully it is blocked at every stage,
because the greater is the unpopularity incurred
by the Government through its failure.
* • *
The want-of-confidence motion which was
introduced the other day in the New South
Wales Assembly differs in no appreciable extent
from the last fifty or sixty similar motions
which have arisen in the various Parliaments of
Australia. It was brought forward, as such
motions almost always are, just when the
Government, despite all other obstacles, was in
danger of accomplishing some useful purpose
whereby it might have gained a certain amount
of credit, and made its position more secure,
and thus kept the Parkes-Reid faction from
getting its turn at the public hog trough as
soon as it expected. There was nothing to be
lost by it, so far as the Foreign-trade party was
concerned, and there was a possibility that
much, in the shape of office and salary,
would be gained. If the attempt succeeded,
the Electoral Bill, the Local Government Bill,
and the Federation Bill would be practically lost
in the confusion, and then the new Foreign-trade
Cabinet would re-introduce these measures as
its own, and attempt to make its billet and re-
election sure by passing them and thus earning
the national gratitude, which gratitude would
otherwise have been wasted on its rivals. If
the attempt did not succeed there was always
a chance that the inevitable loss of time might
lead to some or all of the bills in question being
left unfinished at the end of the session, which
fact would be held up on the pitchfork of
derision as proof of the utter inefficiency of the
Dibbs Cabinet. There was not the remotest
semblance of regard for the public welfare in
the whole miserable business ; it does not pay
to have any regard for the public welfare under
the hideous system of Party Government.
# # *
There is no possible reason to believe that the
evil, bad as it undoubtedly is, has reached
its worst or anything resembling its worst.
Although, thanks to the obstruction and riot
which now pass for statesmanship, such impor-
tant measures as the N.S.W. Local Govern-
ment Bill may hang tire for a quarter of a century
(justas thatbillhasalready hung fire for a quarter
of a century), yet every Cabinet, up to date, has
managed at least to pass one or two new laws,
or to tinker one or two old ones, merely as a
proof of its existence, and has contrived to get
its Estimates put through—generally at the fag
end of the session when it was too late for any
useful criticism. But if the present system
continues it is quite within the bounds of possi-
bility that a time may come when even this will
be found practically impossible—when the Legis-
lature will get down to first principles, and the
process of disintegration will be complete, and
nothing will remain but confusion and blank
anarchy. The system of Party Government
has infinite possibilities of evil and not one
beneficial element. If it lasts representative
government is doomed. It is the road to chaos,
and chaos is the road to .despotism. The only
visible remedy is that which The Bulletin has
so often advocated—the election of Ministers
for a fixed term (say three years) by direct vote
of the House, and altogether irrespective of
parties. The triennial scramble for office might
be bad enough, but at least it would be better
than the daily scramble which prevails at
present. The difference between the two
systems is that between the United States Presi-
dential election, which eventuates every
four years, and a Presidential election which
keeps going on day and night for ever and ever
without amen. The one undoubtedly has its
disadvantages ; the other is built entirely of
disadvantages, and possesses, so far as can be
ascertained by the closest scrutiny, not one
redeeming feature to justify its continuance.
Wanted, a Radical Daily Paper.
In New South Wales Parliament two members
lately accused, with perfect justice, the Sydney
daily papers of habitually suppressing the
speeches delivered by Democratic members of
the local Legislature. As usual, the accused
retorted with insolent sneers at the men who
complained, and who had actually dared to
threaten that, if the practice were continued,
they would retaliate by having attention called
to the presence of strangers, and so exclude
the press whenever their favourites rose to
make speeches. The plea of these dailies, so
far as they condescend to plead at all, instead of
affronting their accusers by alleging that they
never say anything worth reporting, is always
the same —that they supply the public with
what the public wants to get, and that no
newspaper could afford to do otherwise. This
looks quite convincing on the surface, but it
will not bear any careful investigation. How
are the public to know whether or not they
want to hear what Mr. O’Sullivan, Mr.
Black, Mr. Fitzgerald, Mr. Cook, Mr.
Walker, or other members have to say or have
said, if these gentlemen’s speeches are never
reported at all? Judges, just as competent as
the conductors of papers who issue standing
instructions that such and such members are
not to be given more prominence than may be
unavoidable, have informed us that on the
occasion which excited Mr. O’Sullivan’s and
Mr. Fitzgerald’s indignation, Mr. Cook’s
deliverance on Federation (The Bulletin here
invites attention to Hansard) was superior in
weight, in freshness of treatment, and in
lucidity of argument to that which ex-Trea-
surer M'Millan delivered. Now, Mr.
M'Millan’s was reported nearly verbatim,
whereas not one single word of Mr. Cook’s was
published. His views were withheld altogether
from the public, which was curtly informed
that “ Mr. Cook also spoke.” Similarly
we are advised that the oration of
Mr. Walker (who is distinctly the most
highly-endowed with rhetorical gifts of any
member of the New South Wales Legislature)
dealt with the entire series of positions taken
up just before by Sir Henry Parkes in his


sentimental speech, and fairly demolished
position after position with a sequence of
logical treatment and a felicity of language and
of illustration which embellished a deliverance
that reflected honour upon the Assembly
wherein it was uttered. The daily morning
papers gave their readers no hint of this. Mr.
Cook, miner and M.P., does hot often speak—
when he does speak he does invariably speak to
good purpose. Mr. Walker is regarded as a
sort of theological and political outlaw by a
number of people. But a fine oration is a line
oration no matter by whom delivered; and a
sound argument is none the less sound because
it is uttered by a person whom you do not like.
Again, supposing Mr. Cook had not before
made any valuable contribution to the
Assembly’s debates. What would not students
of parliamentary history now give to have a
record of the uureported words of “Single-
speech Hamilton ?”
* * *
But, apart from the stupidity of refusing to
the public a hearing of a good speech under
any circumstances, the plea of business neces-
sity will not hold good. The S.M. Herald,
which on every similar occasion has slunk
behind the cell-door—we mean behind the plea
—is perhaps not aware that its own very
existence is a standing demonstration of ths
falsity of the plea that a newspaper, in
order to prosper, must supply the public
with what the public wants. At no single
period of all its money-making career has the
Sydney Morning Herald ever complied with
its own axiom. During the years when it was
the only morning newspaper of Sydney, it was
defiant and contemptuous of the public tastes.
Even now, when competition prods its fat sides
with spur-pricks, it yields but grudgingly to
the demands of the public, and is dull when
they yearn for brightness, and stupid while they
crave for talent. The fact is "that an old-
established daily newspaper, with a deep-
rooted advertising grip, can for long (though
not for ever) afford to neglect the public tastes
and aspirations to an extraordinary degree.
Men in business must buy the paper wherein
are most concentrated trade announcements
which cannot be equally found elsewhere. The
servant out of work, the employer wanting
servants, the man who lends, the man who
desires to borrow, the person with something
to sell, the person who designs to buy, each and
all must purchase the sheet in which there is
the fullest compendium and collection of that
sort of information. Such persons would con-
tinue to buy— muni continue to buy—even
although the rest of the pages were not merely
surrendered to the maunderings of dulness, but
devoted to themes and opinions which were
most distasteful to them. The S.M. Herald
is in its essentials a trader in advertised in-
formation and has the best and biggest assort-
ment. That is its pecuniary backbone. It
throws in news and comments as secondary
attractions, just as a grocer gives lollies to
customers, or the mammoth grocer distributes
coins hidden in slices of a monster cheese at
—Xmas as an inducement to customers to keep
the profitable part of the trade moving.
» » #
Moreover, in any limited community, when a
couple of journals can divide between thepi the
monopoly of early intelligence, they can create
a demand to suit their own inclinations, if they
happen to be in accord. This is just the posi-
tion in Sydney at the present time. Both
journals have for years boomed the Freetraders
among public men to the diminishment of Pro-
tectionists of equal ability ; and at present both
are concurring in magnifying the Conservative
sort of representatives and in sinking the
Democratic members out of sight. By fulness
of report, or judicious omissions when to report
fully would be injurious to their prothges, by
constantly recurring complimentary comments,
and little puffing pars, they swell out the
importance of particular men until the public
really believe that these artificially inflated
individuals are the real giants among their
representatives. And inversely, by suppression,
tempered by timely sneers, they diminish the
apparent intellectual and political stature of the
men whom they hold in disfavour, till again
their readers fancy that these persons are com-
parative pigmies, incapable of sustained thought
or coherent argument. This accomplished, it
naturally follows that the public becomes
desirous to know when the supposed giant
moves, and is indifferent about the imagined
pigmy. And thereupon the cause of this pre-
ference dishonestly and disingenuously pretends
to merely bow before and minister to it. If an
illustration be required, a single one will serve
to establish the case we have put. No one in
the capital of New South Wales can yet have
forgotten how B. R. Wise was boomed by both
the morning journals into huge political propor-
tions. And no one can have failed to observe
the utter collapse of that gentleman’s bladdery
fame ever since these papers have ceased to
pump oxygen into his political reputation.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“The Big Plea”: by The Little Plea.
The exact nature of the purpose intended to be
served by the interview between Mr. E. J.
Welch, the just-returned ex-private secretary
to Dr. Ren wick, N.S.W. representative at
Chicago, somewhat puzzles Oraculardom. The
interview exhales either a freshness of stupidity
or it steams with spleen. Mr. Welch, who, as
an old pressman, probably understands the
value of puli'—even self-puff—opens out by
giving himself a splendid testimonial: —
“You have ” (wrote Dr. Henwick to his invalided secre-
tary) “been a faithful and useful officer, and to the dis-
charge of your responsible duties you have brought a
whole-hearted loyalty,” &o.
Immediately upon which pleasant and con-
vincing introduction follows an (ironical ?)
apotheosis of the miserable little Renwick,
who has been represented by cable—we now
learn maliciously misrepresented—as coiling
up on the doormat of the Colonial Office
in London whining for a title as a pup
whines for a bone. Mr. Welch’s version
has all the “Jesuitical” flavor of a polemical
romance. He tells us pathetically that Ken-
wick never spoke to him about a prospective
knighthood, until he, in rummaging Renwick’s
despatch-box, discovered therein a letter from
Sir Henry Parkes, who “considered it desir-
able—to emphasise the position which Dr.
Ren wick occupied as representative of New
South Wales—that he should go to Chicago as
‘Sir Arthur Ren wick,’” There was also in
that despatch-box “a copy of a letter from
Lord J ersey to Parkes agreeing thoroughly
with the proposition made by Sir Henry, and
stating that he would write by the outgoing
mail and impress ” upon the authorities at the
Colonial Office the necessity of flinging the Poor
Pup a Bone. When Welch congratulated his
boss as “Sir Arthur,” the modest Renwick
remarked, with deliciously complacent self-
abnegation (in our mind’s eye, Horatio, do we
see his deprecatory hand-wave): —“Nonsense,
it may never be ; I have no personal ambition
for it; but evidently, as you see, Sir Henry
Parkes and Lord Jersey consider that it would
very much improve my position going amongst
those democratic people with a title.” Here
bathos collapses and yells for onions. Imagine
the scene. Poor little Squashsquirt, M.D.,
aspiring to impress “ those democratic people ”
(among whom George Washington himself
went title-less) with a brand-new British
Bladder! Mr. Welch declares, on his say-
so, that Renwick “ didn’t care two straws
about this promised title, except in so far as it
might facilitate his labours on the other side.”
“ Except ! ” Oh, the martyrdom of it ! He
was prepared to sacrifice himself upon the
altar of his suffering country ! They all are !
Every huckster who finds himself by Grad-
grindery, or accident, or otherwise, spitted
aloft on some temporary political skewer,
“ doesn’t care tuppence for a knighthood,” re-
gards it as a hollow and ridiculous bauble ; hut,
should the “bleeding country” call upon him
to be so branded, he is (like Dibbs and Ren-
wick) perfectly prepared to face the awful
ordeal. As if the gibbering patriot would
accept smallpox, or anything that brings no
Bauble and no Loot, on behalf of his “ berlovid
country.” Having apologised for and explained
away Renwick, Welch prompts the reporter—
or the reporter prompts Welch —to take a rise
out of Premier Sir George Republican Dibbs,
the bearer of a “recently conferred distinction
which had only been acquired shortly before
our arrival, and which was the subject of un-
disguised mirth and scornful reference on all
sides.” Welch does not omit to eject a
venomous squirt at the apostate Dibbs’ big
swear in New York, snap-shotting it short off
with all the professional embellishment of a
true photographer. It is easily seen that
Welch cordially dislikes Dibbs. Perhaps he
has good reason for so doing ! But, the question
is—Does Welch also hate Dr. Renwick ? Had
he been the little big-man’s most implacable
enemy, he could not have devised a more
brutally effective method of holding up to
public scorn and derision the insignificant
bauble-hunter who misrepresents at Chicago
Exhibition an Imperialistically - electroplated
country which is democratic at heart.

A Superannuation Swindle.
It has been finally ascertained that the New
South Wales Civil Service Superannuation Fund
is absolutely and utterly insolvent. It has also
been ascertained that the insolvency has been
brought about by the practice of allowing
favoured individuals, blessed with political
influence, to retire on full pension many years
before the prescribed age. This is the delicate
manner in which the report expresses the situa-
tion ; if the document had been put in plain
Australian language it would have set forth
that the fund has been ruined by breach of
trust, fraudulent misappropriation, embezzle-
ment, highway robbery, and bushranging. The
Superannuation Fund was created in order
that every civil servant might receive a pension
at the age of 60, or earlier if permanently
disabled by illness, and that certain small
allowances might be made to any one who lost
his place through retrenchment. The fund is
supported almost entirely by the contributions
of the Service, and the amount of these con-
tributions was calculated to meet the demands
above-mentioned, and nothing more. The
ordinary public servant never, by any pos-
sibility, got more than his bond ; but a
large number, who were blessed with in-
fluence, seem to have retired successfully
as early as the age of 33, with full pension.
Some of them presented medical certificates
slating that they were suffering from incurable
complaints, and then went into private business
and now earn respectable incomes, while at the
same time drawing a liberal allowance from the
fund on the ground of hopeless disablement.
Others seem to have been pitchforked into the
service merely that they might presently be
“ retrenched ” out of it and draw a salary for
doing nothing ever afterwards. The Parkes
Government was by far the greatest sinner in
this respect. It had, of course, no more earthly
right to endow its friends in such a fashion
with the savings of the civil servants than it
had to confiscate the funds of the A.M.P.
Society for their benefit, or to make their pen-
sions chargeable on the reserve fund of the Q.
N. Bank, and the one act is as absolute a rob-
bery as the other. Moreover, the Superannua-
tion Fund is so convenient a means of bribery
that the evil is not likely to end here. The
only positive remedy seems to be that the civil
servants should appoint their own managers
and themselves administer the fund, each con-
tributory having a vote. It is practically an
insurance society ; each civil servant is a policy-
holder ; and the Government has no more claim
to control its affairs than it has to take over by
main force the management of the Colonial
Mutual or any other society, and get drunk
with the cash.
«.
A Lucky Shark,
Last week, Nathan, of the Australian Banking
Co. swindle, was tried for his share in that
monstrous imposture, and being found guilty
the law squashed him with a sentence of nine
months. Nathan, upon the evidence, was the
first cause of the Australian Banking Co., and
about the most brazen and conscienceless
adventurer in the whole gang by whom it was
worked. It was he who created the institution by
selling to it an utterly worthless money-lending
business, and who took a huge amount in paid-
up shares in exchange for the “ good-will ” of a
one-room office with a table in it, two or three
chairs, and a blotting-pad. It was he, also,
who had the stupendous gall to inform the
court that when he performed this swindle he
was a mere youth—an unsophisticated child of
nature—and that he didn’t even know the
meaning of “scrip.” The idea of a Hebrew
money-lender—a man who was occupied in
making advances at usurious interest on
shares, bond - warrants, and every form
of tangible security professing to be
ignorant of the most common term employed in
every-day business is interesting, to say the
least, and it seems almost a pity that the court
didn’t cross-examine the blushing Israelite a
little further to ascertain if he knew what was
meant by ink, or if he had ever heard of
postage-stamps. It was Nathan also who,
when he quitted the Australian Banking Com-
pany, straightway proceeded to found another
bank of much the same character, which has
since burst. It was he, to all appearance, who
led Mathey to his ruin and made him a scape-
goat. Unlike Abigail, who toiled for many
years in his grindery emporium on Brickfield
Hill, Sydney, he seems never to have done one
stroke of honest work in his whole useless
existence; he was by nature a hawk, a financier,
money - lender, pawnbroker, bill-discounter,
pedlar, floater of bogus companies, usurer,
trader in scrip, and unadulterated vampire.
But he had the luck to be tried before one Judge
while Abigail had the ill-luck to be tried
before another; consequently Abigail re-
ceives five years and Nathan exactly nine
months. If the Judges had been re-
versed, the chances are that Nathan
would have got ten years and Abigail
about 26 weeks. There is no system or method
about the administration of justice; the punish-
ment doesn’t fit the crime—it only tits the liver
of the presiding Judge. If sentences were
fixed—as they ought to be—by the jury rather
than by the bench, then the sentence would fit
the jury’s liver instead. Only in the latter
case the sentence would express the mean
derangement of twelve livers instead of the
maximum derangement of one, and by this
device a much better average would be arrived
at. This is why The Bulletin wants juries to
be allowed to fix sentences. No man’s fate
should hang entirely on one liver.
A New Australian Magazine.
In the bright lexicon of Young Australia there is
no such word as name-reverence. Yet here is
a new magazine, the Antipodean (written in
Australia, published in London), well printed,
well illustrated, well got-up generally— hut run on
names. It is edited by George Essex Evans and
John Tighe Ryan, both evidently on the high road
to a glorious Cockney hyphenation. Amongst
the literary contributors are one Countess, three
Honorables (“within the colony”) two knights,
two Cabinet Ministers, one ex-Oabinet Minister.
Some of these days a man with an ordinary name
say, Smith—too ugly or too modest to print his
own portrait in his own publication, and too
level-headed to print the portraits of any adver-
tising nobodies, will arise and make at least a
decent living out of an Australian magazine.
This man Smith will look after the matter and let
the names take care of themselves ; he will jerk
into the waste-paper basket the opoponaxed MS. of
the Duke’s high-collared second cousin Mont-
morenci de Jobkins, and will proceed to apotheo-
size the scraps of tea-paper whereon are traced
the lucubrations of the nameless Bohemian whose
toes protrude from his boots. It is only the slop-
clothes of literature that fit the general-public.
Literary Tailors by Special Appointment to his
Excellency This or That have the best part of
their goods in the gold letters on the big plate-
glass window. This is mainly what is the matter
with the Antipodean : it is made of names.
The Countess of Jersey opens the ball by stating
that the “aim of the Antipodean is to form a
literary link between the Mother Country ” —we
always think of it as the Mother-in-law Country
“ and those of her children who live beyond the
seas.” Despite her ladyship’s silence on the
point, The Bulletin has reason to believe that
another aim is to supply a “ long-felt want.” The
Mother Country and her children, you see, abso-
lutely yearn to know more about good old postur-
ing, much-photographed Sir Henry Parkes, and
he is not backward in supplying the desired
information. The Brummagem bard and martyr
has two sour-grape poems in this number, the first
(“Weary”) addressed to his “Weary, weary,
weary heart,” and the second (“Solace”) also
about himself. Both are mere variants of verses
printed long ago by the writer in “The Beauteous
Terrorist” —the authorship of which latter, by the
way, was first revealed to the world in these
columns —and both ring the changes on the melan-
choly fact which sticks out between the lines that
Dibbs, not Parkes, just now (though it may not
be for much longer) sits in the seat of the N.S.W.
Colonial Secretary. The poet is—
“ Weary of the joys that pain,
Dead-sea fruit whose ashes fall,
Drying up the summer rain,
Charnel dust in cups of gall.”
Also—
“ Wrapt in a robe of thankfulness ”
which we take to be a new sort of poetic pyjama—
I bless the sick bed where I lie,
While thoughts of others sharing less
Of comfort cloud mine aching eye.”
“ Mine aching I!” Thrice-blessed consumma-
tion ! How glad the world will be to find that
Parkes feels his “ I ” ache at last!
It is one of the painful results of being a
“ cauliny ” that when any Australian produces a
piece of decent verse the cognoscenti sniff through
a lordly nose and patronisingly say : “Not bad,
you know, but too suggestive of Bret Harte.”
This is the only fault that Parkes’s poetry hasn’t
got. It isn’t in the least like Bret Harte.
Sir Henry Parkes also writes “ William Charles
Wentworth and John Dunmore Lang: an Imagin-
ary Conversation.” As an effort in the vein of
Lucian, Landor and Traill, it is a painful crudity
and an impotent failure; it is a brilliant success
merely as an exhibition of sordid vulgarity, ludi-
crous self-inflation, littleness, and insolent pre-
sumption on that public ignorance and forgetful-
ness which for 50 years have been the stock-in-
trade of the man from Brummagem. The subject
of the “conversation” is the N.S.W. Upper
Chamber. Parkes (who has filled the Legislative
Council with mere political carrion—a lot of
whom, by the way, subsequently appeared in
public as creditors of his) impudently makes
Wentworth tell about “the ignorant will of a
truckling minister” (name?). He makes Lang
say of the members of the present N.S.W. Counci
that it is better not to enquire into their ante-
cedents —“ the police, lam told, have enough to
do.” He makes Wentworth ask; “Had our
compatriots no sons?” and Lang reply; “The
thought is bitter. What is his name who occupies
your chair in the model Upper Chamber?” The
name, as a name, is unfortunate; but what of
that? “No Aristides, no Alfred, no Washing-
ton ? ” enquires Wentworth. Certainly not:
there has been too much Parkes.
* # #
Sir Samuel Griffith* who now obviously sees
the hideous mistake his political life has been,
squirms and wriggles characteristically on “The
Coloured Labour Question in Australia.” “The
continuance of Polynesian immigration,” he now
soothingly tells us, “can neither be permanent nor
assume large proportions.” The Northern Judas
may be safely left to stew slowly in his own gravy.
W. O. Hodgkinsou (has anyone of the present
generation ever read the lovely but unclean scien-
tific hoax he palmed off on the poor unsuspecting
clergy of the S. M. Herald in the long ago?)
writes a pleasant little story concerning the matri-
monial adventures of one Ah Kim. Miss Lilian
Blair grows melancholy about a Samoan girl,
Solita, who wanted to dance but died instead.
The melancholy is tempered to the sympathising
reader by a picture of a very pretty little Samoan
which stands at the beginning of the story and is
called after Miss Blair. What has happened to Miss
Jennings Carmichael, who but the other day wrote
so neat and swinging an “In Memoriara” to
Tennyson, that she should give us two such lines
as these in a set of verses ?
Lift red to my lips your bubbling wine,
Quickening the physical pulses fine !
* # #
J. B. O’Hara’s “A Forest of the Dead ” is, as
a piece of literature, perhaps the best thing in the
book, but it might be a great deal better (or
possibly somewhat worse) if Mr. O’Hara didn’t
know his Gordon and Kendall and Marcus Clarke
so well. By the way, Mr. O’Hara seems to think
that “ warrigal ” means a wild dog. We have
never heard the term so used, but have taken it as
applying to wild cattle or horses only. In Queens-
land it has been used, like “ myall,” to denote a
wild blaekfellow. Mr. H. J. Taperell writes of
“The Australian (Titheradge and ‘Bob’ Brough
and George Rignold!) Drama Mr. Price Fletcher
has something about Australian scenery. “Types
of Australian Beauty ” consist of a reproduction
of eight ladies’photographs. The first lady (the
eight are unnamed, but are all tolerably well-
known in “society”) is distinctly pretty, and so,
in perhaps a less degree, are several of the
others; but one of the group looks 15 if
a day, and two certainly have (or is it that they
ought to have?l a half-set of artificial teeth each.
We fear that Messrs. Evans and Ryan have been
selecting their beauties as they apparently pick
their contributors —on their names and addresses.
Mr. Evans writes a “ Song of Life,” founded on a
basis of solid and eternal fact, and accompanied
by a portrait of the gifted author, with sad, weird
eyes gazing out into the immensities of the Here-
after through the infinite heart-yearnings of the
Now. The veteran David Blair turns up smiling,
spectacled, and reminiscent as of yore, in “ The
Night of the Grand Debate.” But what’s the
matter with the real names of the gentlemen who
figured on that occasion ? Mr. John Tighe Ryan
writes “Ab6ut Interviewing his victims,
Parkes, Max O’Rell, and H. M. Stanley.
Jennings Carmichael has an interesting little
sketch, “The Children of the Bush,” with an
admirable mechanical illustration, “Gin and
Piccaninny.” Grace Channing Neill has firm
ideas about “The Antipodean Girl.” Accord-
ing to Mrs. Neill, “the. native daughter of
this sun-loved land ” is “ slight in figure,
small-featured, with delicate complexion and
“tiny hands and feet” (11 1). Also when she
is a bush girl she “fears neither man nor horse.” As
an expression of frantic recklessness “ fearsjneither
God nor horse ” would be a lot more comprehen-
sive. Before she marries, the Australian girl is
“ piquante, bright, and pretty,” and has a “wil-
lowy slouch.” “Willowy slouch” is distinctly
good. After she is married she “ gyrates’twixt
Rabies and bed.”

Harry I. Blake is the best-looking man in the
book. There is nothing else remarkable about his
Kanaka sketch. “ Philip Dale ” writes about
Jenny who loved a bold stockman but married
somebody else and half promised to run away with
the bold stockman, whose unhallowed proposals
were overheard by the somebody else. This
cunning individual tells Jenny that she can run if
she likes, whereupon Jenny, of course, doesn’t
like, and “ with a sigh she drew his arms about
her and pressed her head upon his breast, as upon
a rock of strength and goodness.” The story stops
right here, and there is reason to believe that
Jenny stays with the old man. Henry Lawson
has a story—not quite up to his standard—about
“Christmas in the Far West,” and about a man
(whom he curiously enough calls a “solitaire”)
humping a corpse about. 0. E. Robin,
8.A., who evidently knows his subject,
writes in a patronising and humorous way
about “The Antipodean Schoolboy.” “ Chris-
tophus” has a story about my “Gulf Hel-
met.” It is founded on fact, and supported
by Christophian imitations of Jerome K. Jerome’s
style of humour. For a taste: “ After nearly
knocking down six people and injuring a lamp-
post I took off my helmet and walked home with
it in my hand. I was not afraid of sunstroke
because my head was amply protected by three
largo bumps.” When Christophus does get hold
of a good thing he sticks to it. The Antipodean
is most excellently illustrated—mainly by Messrs.
Frank Mahoney, Percy Spence, and G. E. Minns.
It is published by Messrs. George Robertson and
Co. at the popular price of a shilling.

When does it cease to be mutinous to dis-
obey the commands of a man no longer capable of
giving rational orders? “Never,” gays the
N.S.W. “Dead Marine” Board.
It is now, after the event, easy to see
that the officers of the Port Glasgow erred
in tactics by actively participating in the peti-
tion by the crew, after the hurricane, for the
displacement of the captain. The officers should,
as a mere question of dodgery, have remained
neutral. They would have had to supersede the
skipper just the same, and then when the matter
came up for trial in port, they would have been
three unchallengeable witnesses whose evidence
would have settled the facts one way or other.
But as they threw in their lot with the men there
were no witnesses-all the parties were, from a
red-tape point of view, involved on one side or
other in the dispute. All the same, sailors, and
not merely captains, should be represented on the
N.S. W. Marine Board, which, now, is a purely
aristocratic, hodv.
