Premier Dibbs, in the alleged interests of
propriety and morality, has stopped the exhibition
of the lady lion-tamer at Fillis’ Circus, and
though that harassed female called and remon-
strated, Dibbs, K.O.M.G. (the lady-tamed Re-
publican lion), presented a cast-iron front and
refused to shift his mental attitude. Dibbs
is hereby requested to explain his position. There
is nothing immoral in a lady being eaten up; there
is nothing in it that can even be called improper.
It may be painful to be bitten to death by a lion,
but a lady of the most brass-bound virtue may go
through the process without a blush. There is
nothing about the business, either, that is specially
demoralising to the spectators ; the crowd that
attends a lion-show is not nearly so far down as
that which haunts a prize-fight, or a dog-fight, or
which tries to get admission to a hanging, or that
haunts the gaol on flogging-day. The business is
dangerous, no doubt, but if the lady is willing, it
is distinctly her funeral and nobody else’s. The
right to do oneself an injury is the inalienable
privilege of every free citizen, whether the self-
injury is done by drink, opium, lion-bite, accept-
ance of a K.C.M.C.-ship, over-exertion, loss
of character through mean equivocation con-
cerning an item of <£looo for travelling expenses,
the publication of poems which cast a doubt
on the writer’s sanity, or any other foolishness.
Moreover, it is a piece of ghastly meanness to
deprive any woman of her means of livelihood.
Unless Dibbs is prepared to put a vote on the
estimates to recompense the lion-female for her
loss, he stands self-convicted of boycotting and
intimidation of a helpless woman to prevent her
following her lawful calling, also he is out on
strike and is a dog in the manger, and is dis-
locating the wheels of industry, and is preventing
the investment of capital—in the purchase of
lions—and is guilty of an outrage against human
freedom, inasmuch as he won’t either do the work
himself on union principles or allow anybody else
to do it by blackleg labour. The Bulletin is
therefore of opinion that Premier Dibbs should be
tried by a Deniliquin jury and get two years.
** * *
Wirth’s Circus —it begins to be an open
question if there isn’t too much circus about—
opens on December 23, at the York-street rink,
Sydney. The show is purely Australasian, barring
sundry Arabs and Japanese, and therefo-e it
appeals strongly to the patriotic soul of A'oung
Australia as he blasphemes with his shilling at the
side entrance. Also, it is a large sliow with an
old-established reputation, and when it bursts
forth in all its glory nothing but the pallid fingers
of Death can stop it as a financial spec.
** * *
That grisly image of decay, Sydney Opera
House, has brightened up wonderfully under the
Harry Rickards regime, and on Saturday nights
a board with the strange device, “standing room
only,” may be seen at each door, also the same
weird legend turns up about twice during the
week on an average. Nobody—not even the very
oldest of all inhabitants-can remember when
there was standing room only at the Opera House
before. Mr. Rickards has gathered a good com-
pany together, and, with the sagacity of an old
campaigner, he only allows the niggers to pro-
pound one conundrum each during the evening.
Also, he “knocks ’em in the old Kent Road”
with the same leery affability as before, and his
coster sketches are encored six times on an
average. This boundless enthusiasm of an Austra-
lian audience over the coster of the old Kent Road
is a matter which wants explanation. There are
no weak points in the company excepting the pale-
green lady in the dress that hangs straight down
like a green cataract. Johnny Gilmore is worth
special mention as one of the few'*really funny
niggers on record, and Miss Katherine Vinceni,
the lady violinist, is a huge success. The Aus-
tralians may not be prepared to devour great
masses of music at once, but when they get a
little classical melody between two blackfellows’
jokes their enthusiasm is something to be remem-
bered. Also, the “ Ta-ra-ra-boom-de-ay ” dance
by a chorus of eight ladies, with sixteen feet all
flying in the air at once, and sixteen stockings
conspicuously in evidence, and eight back-hairs
coming down simultaneously, seems to fill an
aching void and to supply a long-felt want.
** * «
The principal Christmas gorgeousness at
Fillis’ Circus is to be the water pantomime—
-50,000 gallons of the fluid which lies heavy on the
stomach being poured into the arena “in full view
of the audience.” This is what the advertisement
says, though 50,000 gallons of anything couldn’t
well be poured in without the audience noticing it
in any case. The pantomime will be conducted
by 100 artists in canoes, steam launches and skiffs,
and the clown will make his observations from a
raft, and the pantaloon will hold forth in a" punt,
and the fairy chorus will arrive by ketch and will
feed on ship-biscuit and salt-horse to lend an air of
realism to the proceedings, and the wicked sprite
will be located on a coal-hulk, and will shiver her
timbers and so on. Also the rest of the company
■will dash their tarry top-lights.
The other theatres show signs of tremend-
ous activity. At Her Majesty’s “Blue Beard”
is to be put on with even more than the usual
glory of costumes and scenery, and in this
particular variety of gorgeousness George Rignold
stands almost unrivalled. The Lyceum opens
with a burst of magnificence in the shape of
“ Little Red Ridinghood.” Misses Alice Leamar,
Cora Tinnie, Addie Conyers, and Messrs. Robert
Courtneidge, Bert Haslem, Slade Murray, the
Walhalla Family, and others have been specially
engaged, also G. L. Goodman is business manager.
At the Garrick, Fred B. Norton (take the
elevator) promises a season of comedy, with Tom
Cannam as leading comedian. Brough and
Boucicault have taken the favourite Criterion for
six months, and open on Monday with “ Much
Ado About Nothing.” All the old Criterion
company will take a hand, with Sass and the ever-
green Harwood thrown in as additional glories
without any extra charge. Also sundry minstrel
shows and other small fry are to be heard of in
various places.
** * *
Lonnen, according to a somewhat maudlin
yarn in the Argus, wept copiously when the
news that he had “gone up one” in his line of
business was telephoned to the Princess’s. Imme-
diately after curtain-fall on the Wednesday night
there came a pathetic tinkle of the telephone-bell.
“Fred Leslie’s dead,” spake a voice from the dis-
tance, whereupon the person listening at the
Princess’s machine dropped the “ receiver ” with
a moan, and the sad tidings echoed through the
theatre. An Argus man, who seems to have been
feasting his eyes upon Lonnen at the time, saw
“ tears trickle down the grease paint ” on his face,
and will never forget the harrowing spectacle. It
is not every journalist who retains such a beautiful
faith in theatrical grief. Most professional theatre-
goers have discovered that actors are prone to
“breakdown” in this manner, and can always
choke at the right time. A disposition to
be “ overcome with emotion ” is noticeable
in every stage artist when he makes a benefit
speech. An affecting sob is a part of his stock-in-
trade which he displays to the best effect on all
suitable occasions. Doubtless Lonnen was sorry
to hear of his friend’s death, but he had no more
natural cause for weeping than has any sym-
pathetic commercial man who learns that an old
pal is no more. Actors, moreover, are not in-
different to the question of salary, and Lonnen,
even while the dew was on his grease paint, could
scarcely help feeling that the demise of a powerful
rival had suddenly improved his own price on the
market. One is rather tired of the old gag about
the down and his private sorrows anyway, and
in this particular sorrow of Lonnen for the loss of
Leslie worldlings will find more artificial hysteria
than genuine human nature.
*m * *
The possibility of Melbourne being left
unprovided with drama for a whole week was
obviated by a sudden opening of the Opera House
on Saturday last. A band of patriots, announced
as “under entirely new management,” took pos-
session of the premises, and burst forth in a Xmas
revival of “Current Cash.” A lurid Major, upon
whose disingenuous brow a pot-hat is jauntily
poised, seems to be the most mysterious feature of
“ Current Cash.” According to the programme
this man is “ treacherous and base”—and he looks
it. Also, he acts according. He makes a practice
of confessing his sins and wickedness to the foot-
lights, and his method of scowling upon a
bottle-nosed blackmailer (who once caught him
in the act of forging a will) is horrible to
contemplate. In the course of the even-
ing another man disguised as the Fifth
of November comes along to haunt the
Major, which causes him to grind his teeth to
slow music, and discuss with himself the pro-
priety of clearing everybody from his path. A
widow and her daughter, having been reduced to
shirt-making by the forged will, beg for mercy
from the Major; also his delicately-nurtured
niece asks him not to force her to marry a tame
parson, but the Major is obdurate all round. A
colder-blooded ruffian never made faces at the pit.
The present writer has grown old in contemplat-
ing a long line of miscreants who revel in their
infamy, and he now desires to give the Major the
cake. Fortunately, however, the Guy Fawkes
person with a dissembled voice turns out to be
the captain that had his will forged in the first
act, and this generous joke, taken in conjunction
with the timely repentance of the bottle-nosed
interloper, and other unexpected circumstances,
seems to bring the Major to grief. For such as
delight in seeing retribution fall upon the
unrighteous with a sickening thud there are worse
places than the Opera House. Moreover, if they
called at several other theatres just now they
would only find the door shut. “Current Cash”
is up to the average of desperate theatrical
ventures, and the numerous company engaged are,
no doubt, anxious to live. Some of them oughtn’t
to live long, ’tis true, but Fred Carnbourne gives
an amusing sketch of the low comedy boozer, and
Alf. Boothman deserves praise for a successful
attempt at hiding his identity every now and
then.
The Melbourne pantomimes, as it happens, will
start to knock one another out, if possible, on
Christmas Eve. At the Princess’s they are said
to have prepared a costly setting of “The Forty
Thieves.” This show, which inaugurates the new
partnership of Williamson and Musgrove, is
expected to be worthy of the occasion. Lonnen,
Alice Lethbridge, Katie Barry, Lelia Roze, and a
long list of bright visions, are billed to appear as
Thieves, or otherwise, and the rorty Snazelle has
been fetched over by cable from the Cannibal
Islands to play a congenial part.
*■ * * *
The close of the B. and B. management
on Friday was followed by an extra special per-
formance to the honour and benefit of Messrs.
Brough and Boucicault on Saturday last. A
densely crowded house, at an advanced scale of
prices, assembled to see the last of the old regime.
“ The Colonel ” gave the company an opportunity
to contribute their valuable services, after which
Lonnen, Stockwell, Theo. Liebe, Alice Lethbridge,
and Snazelle did each a good turn for the late
lessees. Snazelle, by the way, recited a piece
from the works of one Sydney Bulletin. Finally,
Brough and Boucicault delivered valedictory
addresses in two well-contrasted styles—the
emotional and the matter-of-fact. Stockwell
struck up “For they are jolly good fellows,” and
“ Hip-hip-hip !” was the cue for the curtain to
fall.

In tones adapted to descriptions of heroic
deeds The Bulletin mentions that the Alexandra
was reopened, on Monday last, by Arthur Vivian.
To open the Haleck now-a-days is a solemn thing,
inasmuch as the house has an oyster-like mania
for shutting up suddenly. “The Woman of the
People ” introduces the new management to
notice, and “a dramatic constellation” is the
term applied to the talent laid on.

A Santa Claus festival, taking the form of
a gigantic musical oorroboree and a transcendant
wood-chopping tournament, promises to strike
out a new' line in booms at Melbourne Exhibition
Building. The orchestra and brass-band portion
of the festival will commence to shake the time-
honoured welkin on Saturday night, while the
first round of tiie great axe-fight is reserved for
Boxing afternoon. The music-halls wave their
holiday programmes in the Xmas air, and Jere-
miah Brown has arranged to stay and see the
Waterloo Cyolorama through its positively last
month in Melbourne. Jeremiah was 101 years of
age at latest dates, but his personal recollections
of Wellington, Buonaparte, arid the Old Guard
are more extraordinarily vivid than ever.

Cora Urquhart Potter and Kyrle Bellew send Christmas
greetings and two beautiful “gem” portraits from
Washington (U.S.A.) to a Sydney writer. Cora signs the
letter for both. That’s all right—these twain are one.
“Therese Raquin,” in the hands of Mrs. Potter and her
Bellew, has taken America by storm. The book is Zola’s
most gruesome, and, according to the critics, the play
goes one better. Cora undresses herself on the stage right
down to a nightgown.
Cunningham, who is running an Australian yabber-
yabber-cMm-boomerang show in 'Frisco, writes dolefully
to a Townsville (Q.) pard. The blaokfellers have got flash
and lazy: “Coming over in the steamer there were
several missionaries, and they went into the steerage and
put everything that was bad into the blacks’ heads.”
Cunningham must be a poor showman. Why didn’t he
offer the saints a substantial commission, and lard the

Commissioner Dr. Renwiok, passing through London en
route to Chicago, visited Westminster Aquarium with his
daughters and private secretary. The doctor fidgeted
thro’ the main part of an excellent variety programme,
till Miss Lottie Lightfoot appeared in the Serpentine

There was a rush to Melbourne Princess’s on the night
following the announcement of Fred Leslie’s death. An
idea that the girls would sport tights of sable hue had
circulated abroad, and many of the audience evidently
anticipated a display of sentiment. Nothing unusual
occurred, though. Burlesque doesn’t offer opportunity
for suggestions of sorrow, or perhaps the sorrow had had
time to abate.
Fred Leslie died of typhoid fever, a disease which lurks
in sewer-gas and such things to capture men who have
“ lived high.” It was probably the effect of bad drains,
acting upon a constitution attenuated by good drains,
that removed poor Fred. His death, following upon
Nellie Parren’s break-up, sets one thinking of those cafd
dinners in Melbourne and Sydney, whereat a party of
four would “put away” some seven or eight bottles of
wine and spirits without turning a hair.
The burlesque artist can be guaranteed to make “sym-
pathetic reference” to his rival at a minute’s notice—
when the rival’s dead, not before.

The craze for the manufacture of little Alice Leth-
bridges and Leamars goes on in Melbourne, where the
rival tutrices have just held their displays, counting dolls
by the hundred. This preposterous and demoralising
vanity, amid the upper ten, draws crowds. A big crop of
Countess Uussells is being raised.
Leslie must have been more than 35, the age set down
to him in some notices. He was no longer a boy 15 or 16
years ago, when he played Lantier in a travestie of
“ Drink,” at London Royalty. The writer remembers
him as a slim, serious-looking young man of, perhaps, 24,
dancing and singing just a little with Marie de Grey, and
not seeming to take much interest in a stupid part.

Dolaro, long since dead, appeared in the “dual role ” of
Gervaise and manageress of the show.

Spong, the Bijou scenic artist, off to England, has just
auctioned all his Melbourne traps. Here is a severe loss
to art in this part. Spong excels in witchery of land-
scape, and is allied to Gordon in the best application of
his powers, while Hennings, Brunton, and Goatcher
appear rather stronger, especially Hennings, on the
architectural side.
Spagnoletti: Think of the scenic artists who have
figured in Melbourne Christmas pieces—Opie, Holmes,
Pitt, Tannett, Hennings, Wilson, Freyberger, Habbe,
Gordon, Spong. Brunton, Goatcher. A glorious muster-
roll of talent, with a few sparks of genius. What a
prodigious amount, what acreages, of magnificent scenes,
have been coolly painted out, after serving, in many
cases, only for the background of a stupendous failure,
like Miln’s “Antony and Cleopatra!”

Riley man is a leading shareholder of Sydney

are others of Riley’s political kidney on the Tele-
graph board. The Telegraph is a “ democratic ”
paper. But figs don’t grovy on thistles, and that
which men own, whether it’s a newspaper or a
brothel, will always bear the marks of their
ownership.

The Wild Cat Column.
A certain busted boomer has protested to the
depositors of his institution that neither he nor his wife
has a private hoard. Anyhow, that lady distinctly stated,
only a couple of years ago, at the committee meeting of a
hospital that “ whatever happened to , she was
well provided for.” And that in the presence of about 15
witnesses.

Editor Bulletin, —A leading North Queensland
softgoods Arm consists of three partners, headed by a pietist
of an evidently superior kind—he practically possesses
a, church of his own. The other two are earnest workers
in the Cause—both preach, and one is a Sunday-school
superintendent. They draw between them £6OOO a year
in screw, and last year divided £lB,OOO, besides making
£15,000 by a side wind. And here is where the employes
come in. The window-washer (married, with a family), by
way of a Christmas-box has just been told that 12J per
cent, is to be taken off his wages, which have hitherto
stood at 325, 6d. a week. —O.

Brisbane telegram, unique in its immensity of
chilled-steel cheek:—“Mr. Hodgkinson has intimated
that he is still doing all that is possible with the view of
inducing a syndicate to construct a land-grant railway
from Croydon We will next hear about
the difficulty which Hodgkinson finds in “ inducing ” the
alligator to accept a large piece of raw meat, and the
trouble he experiences in persuading the shark to take
in a little sea-mullet or pork refreshment. Tub Bulletin
hereby invites tenders for a fancy picture of Minister
Hodgkinson doing his utmost to induce that syndicate to
make its own railway and be paid for it in land about
eight times over and then keep the line all the same. The
lowest or any tender not necessarily accepted.
* * *
The synopsis of the accounts of the Anglo-Aus-
tralian Bank (Melbourne), furnished by the Crown Prose-
cutor in opening the case against the directors, reveals
some remarkable figures. Of the 200,000 shares “sub-
scribed,” it is alleged that Staples, the chairman of
directors, took up 192,467 and the other directors took
the balance. One man paid up £37 10s. on his shares ;
the others simply debited themselves with the price of
their shares in the books, so that the aforesaid £37 10s.
was the entire capital of the institution. This w'as the
first issue. Of the second issue, consisting of 50,000
Shares, the chairman took 7000, and a clerk (salary, £3 a
week) took the other 43,000. Neither of them paid a
cent, both being debited with the price of the shares in
the usual manner. The profits were absolutely fictitious ;
the reserve fund was a myth ; the capital was non-
existent ; and the business of the “ bank ” seems to have
consisted entirely of bogus sales to, and bogus purchases
from, another “ bank ” of the same character, and in the
receipt of deposits. Even the “ cash in hand ” is said to
have consisted almost entirely of Staples’ I O U for £7600
—in fact, pretty nearly all the assets were Staples’ IO U
or the I O U’s of other directors.
* ft ft
The trial of the Anglo-Australian bank directors
has been postponed until February. The Victorian
Crown Law Department kindly consented to this arrange-
ment for delaying the possible creation of a judicial
precedent that might make things look awkward for a
pal of the Government.
* • *
N.S.W. Full Court has finally decided, by a
two-thirds majority (Chief Justice Barley and Justice
Manning) that the Anglo-Australian Investment Co., of
Sydney, was justified in going into the hotel business with
the shareholders’ and depositors’ money. The minority
(consisting of Judge Stephen) very pertinently wants to
know what there is now to prevent the directors of any
“bank” or land company or building society running a
theatre or a circus or a music-hall with the deposits, if
they happen to feel that way. Apparently they can run
all three if they want to. The public would like to hear
Judge Windeyer’s opinion upon the subject—before the
case goes to the Privy Council.
• * *
The Liberator Building Society, which smashed
a few months ago in England, now takes rank as the boss
swindle of the decade. The total funds of the concern
were £3,313,000, and of this amount £2,078,445 were lent to
one director, without any security of the slightest value.
Also, £481,950 were lent—likewise without security—to
another society, now burst, in which that director was
interested, and a third society, which is also insolvent,
got £627,000. There was no tangible security in this
case either ; in fact, the directors practically divided over
three millions among them. The present situation is—
liabilities, about £3,500,000 ; assets, supposed to be some-
where between £lO,OOO and £50,000. One director and
the society’s solicitor are in gaol; another director has
resigned his seat in the Commons, and his arrest is
daily expected. Meanwhile the usual fatherly article
about the recklessness of Australian finance appears in
the English papers with its wonted regularity.

Dear Bulletin,—One or two facts to prove that
mining business has not absolutely collapsed in Tasmania.
A month ago Dr. Gorger, the largest shareholder in the
Silver King 00. (Zeehan), cabled out to sell his shares. In
a week or two I placed them all locally, over £lOOO worth,
and rose the market on the deal. A few days ago 5600
Silver Queen shares, held in reserve, were to be
allotted to shareholders. Over 12,000 were applied for.
Early next year there will be half-a-dozen large concen-
trating plants running at Zeehan. The Silver King,
Silver Queen, British Zeehan, Mount Zeehan (Launces-
ton), and Western have all their plants ordered or under
way. As our West Coast silver lead ore is of such high
grade in lead, ranging from 30 to 50 per cent., even if
silver has a further fall, it should still pay to work. —
Yours, &0., Theodore Bevah (member Hobart Stock
Exchange).
# # »
Broken Hill Proprietary mine calls tenders for
enormous lots of coke and timber. High time the N.S.W.
coke-makers cut in for their big market —though the
conditions and therefore the odds are against them.
Timber-getters are cut out by one word—-Oregon—and
why, the writer hardly knows. Can it be shown that for
mining purposes the soft and, therefore, weak timbers of
America are better to hold up ground than the best of
Australian hardwoods? When asked why they used soft
woods, an Australian manager replied that he assumed
that somebody must own the forests in which they grew.
# * *
A miner, who obviously understands his business,
writes in strong condemnation of the management of a
Peak Hill mine. He enumerates blunders and miscal-
culations perpetrated in the making of dams where there
is no water-catchment, and in the erection of useless
machinery. As most N.S.W. gold mines are badly
managed, it would be remarkable if this one were not in
the category.
* * «
Another man lets day into the working of Wesb
Sunlight mine, Hillgrove. A month ago the battery was
started with much jubilation. The 10 head, it was
declared, were only an instalment, power enough having
been provided to run 40 head ; and there were to be no
more calls. But, in a few weeks, there is a mysterious
five-hour daily stoppage of the 10 head, some say to get
up steam ; and another, the tenth, call has been made.
How much of the many calls have been paid—how many
shares are really alive for calls, and how many have
perished by forfeiture? 7s. 6d. a share has been paid on
the contributing shares; a heap of cash and paid-up
shares went to the promoters.
What has become of the New Hargreaves mine?
It has the usual N.S.W. mine history. Struck a rich
patch, selected specimens displayed in Sydney, much
newspaper-puffing. Result: a few sales of shares at high
prices, patch worked out; and now we have reached the
stage when a breathless public are told “ The pumps are
working well.”

■ The “discoverer” of Queensland’s biggest

Was the claim salted, and, if so, by whom?

Big things were one time predicted for Butcher’s
Reef and the surrounding claims. The writer dissented.
Time works wonders for the man who “jumps” on a
mine. Butcher’s Reef “ went ahead so did Bear Hill,
w'hich is near it. £lO,OOO were spent in plant, and over
double in mining w ork. Shares changed hands by the
thousand, and a big comer put Bear Hills up to pounds
apiece. Sans a corner Butcher’s Reef only went to 10s.—
they were never worth as many farthings. Bear Hill
went into decent liquidation—hut poor Butcher’s was
lately seized and sold by the Sheriff for a miserable £llO.
Thus endeth, &o.

The published list of Newcastle collieries form-
ing the N.S.W. United Collieries Co. (capital £3,000,000)
clearly proves that it is simply a combination of nearly
all the small pits with the object of better enabling com-
petition with the bigger ones, such as Wallsend and the
A. A. Co., who will have nothing to do with it.

* * *
Cabled that the Earl of Kilmorey, at a recent
meeting of the Wentworth Proprietary, held in London,
stated that the advising board in Sydney had misled the
shareholders, but since the local management was changed
the profits were at the rate of £60,000 a year. A rich
discovery, such as the owners of the Wentworth mine are
now enjoying, will place the halo of glory around the
worst of management, while a period of barrenness will
condemn the best. At the same time the mine is now
worked on a much better system and is well but expen-
sively managed ; the writer only hopes that the run of
good gold will continue to justify it in the eyes of the
British owners.
* * #
The bulk of the Wentworth gold is contained
in rich arsenical pyrites, which are shipped to Freiburg
for treatment. It is carefully bulk-assayed here, and it
was in this direction that the advisory board made the
mistakes referred to, in London, the other day.

846 Maoquarle-street North, Sydney, Dec. 21,1892,
My Dear Moorabinda, —
Here you have a statement of what Lady Tomnoddy
really expects from her liege subjects. lam told, on
excellent authority, that the wife of an Australian Go-
vernor confided to somebody when she got ’Ome that the
Australians are the queerest people she ever met in the
matter of salutations, “ for at public gatherings, where
we were seated on a dais—all the people passing would
stare at us but very few would respect our presence by
bowing —indeed, only those who knew us personally.”
“ Respect our presence by bowing ” is indeed beautifully
English.
The opening of the Nurses’ Home in connection with
the Alfred (named after Dr. Alfred Roberts) Hospital, the
other Tuesday, is like a phantom fashion-plate viewed at
this distance. Lady Jersey (chaperoned by brother
Rupert No. 2, who lets his eye-glass dangle when there’s
nobody looking) officiated—firstly unveiling a bust of Sir
Alfred Roberts in the main hall of the hospital—evidently
inspired (I mean the bust) when the veteran medicine-man
wasn’t so interestingly furrowed and crow’s-footed as he
is now. I suppose it’s meant as a compliment to turn
out a sculpture with a smooth, unmarked countenance as if
the model had spent a lotos-eating life of inertia, or else
had lit on a genuine wrinkle-destroyer.
The Nurses’ Home—a substantial structure with its
necessarily barrack-like air mitigated by a very feminine
drawing-room with a piano and the walls hung with
artistic gift-pictures—was opened from the balcony by
Lady Jersey, looking as smart as a new pin in a marbled
silk with diagonal lines of blue and pink tiny flowers. Her
ladyship’s opening speech was audible and apropos, but
the vice-regal lack of confidence quite spoilt the effect. If
you want to really grasp how far from reposeful is Lady
J. on the stump just watch the eccentric gyrations of the
umbrella somebody inevitably holds over her if the ordeal
is in the open air. The new Admiral and Adrairaless were
tooled out to the opening by their flag-lieutenant. Mrs.
Bovvden-Smith is a tall, jolly-looking woman with dark
hair and a florid complexion, who scrutinises objects of
special interest through a double-glass and seems Inclined
to be dressy. She sported a black and mauve pintc shot
silk spotted with pink flowers, the bodice made with yoke
and half-sleeves of pink silk veiled in black lace ; lace
shoulder-frill and bands of black moire ribbon piped
with pink (the Princess of Wales has made ribbons the
rage, by the way) round the waist; black lace and jet
bonnet with pink feathers. Miss Bowden-Smith was flop-
hatted and floral.
The engaged youth ought to have eyes in the back of
his head, if he wants to make quite sure that he is the
only swain his Angelina leans upon in the gloaming.
Somebody tells me a conversation he overheard between
two actors in a Sydney caf6 the other day which goes a
long way to prove the soundness of Alice Leamar’s
refrain, “For you can’t trust young ladies entirely, you
know.” Said Mummer the First, “You ought to look
blue, old chappie. I hear your little sweetheart is going
to be married." “Well,” said Mummer No. 2, with a
comprehensive wink, “do you think that makes any
difference ?”
But you can’t trust young gentlemen, either. A
Sydney scholastic individual was recently run down in
his trespasses and sins—as a matter of detail, he had
married two wives. But he didn’t neglect either of them
—he sent regular remittances to his English love, and
dragged a red herring over the trail by telling his local
spouse that he was only doing the dutiful to his aged
mother ! What a pity that such a domestic genius should
ever have been found out! It’s difficult enough to find a
husband who can support one wife nowadays, and so it seems
like wasting good material to convict a man who is ready
and willing to keep two going. O, loving schoolmaster,
I salute thee. Ready am I, almost, to become Number
Three.
A number of young “bankers” congregated in Rush-
cutter’s Bay Oval, the other day, and played cricket—
married v. single—to an afternoon-tea accompaniment.
I didn’t go. There’s no novelty nowadays in seeing a
bank-clerk bowled out.
It really isn’t a gaudy thing at all to be a man. Men
get all the outside seats and all the draughts and do most
of the paying. In a Sydney parsley-hunt to be heard in
February, the wife seeks to shake off her husband, a
young and clever doctor, well-known in Melbourne, who
plays the fiddle divinely, and the Doc. has been ordered
to pay over funds to enable her to carry on the case.
“ Write me out a cheque, Henry, I’m going to apply for a
divorce,” any wife may say, and if he doesn’t pay up the
law will make him. And yet women-suffragists want to
give the sexes an equal chance 1
The discovery that passages of most doubtful “morality’’
were being analysed by students in the recent junior
exams, at Sydney ’Varsity throws a lurid light on the
sweet girl graduate. In future the damsel whose maternal
parent has interdicted Zola will simply “ go up for the
junior.” What a little innocent the girl who got A for
English literature in this year’s scramble must be !
Miss Maggie Moore and Mrs. Lionel Rignold had a front
seat at the amateur performance of “ Caste,” at Waverley,
on 14th inst., and Miss Moore was deeply absorbed in
seeing for the very first time, from the front, a play in
which she had frequently disported as Polly. The ordi-
nary amateurs’ Esther, D’Alroy and the Marquise were
the usual thing. Esther was a lady who would have been
invaluable in a row over the back-fence, and galvanised
broomsticks would have played as well as the other two.
But the youngsters were very interesting. Professional
opinion was that young Titheradge always looked like his
pa—young Anson sometimes, and Emery was like the
juvenile apparition of his father. As for the first two,
their acting was a fair reproduction of the methods of
their sires. To my mind, that was the worst feature of
the show. Neither young Tith. nor Anson, jun., had a
scrap of original business, though, perhaps, the latter, in
the last act, was accidentally a little more original in his
dressing than pa ever attempted to be. Young Emery’s
Genidge was a convincing performance
I am told, on undoubted authority, that Lord Jersey
has one great Australian ambition—to help in catching a
big shark. The overtures of anybody in a position to
allow his Ex. to take part in the capture of a fourteen"
foot blue pointer will be received in a most friendly spirit
at Government House.
All the hoarse-voiced little Sydney newsboys were, last
Saturday, entertained at tea by the popular Quong Tart—
after first having paraded the city with a band and many
banners. Swarmed round the tables they looked very
poor and ragged, but their faces were clean, and their
behaviour wonderfully good. At the end of the feast one
youngster, unasked, chivalrously helped the waitresses to
clear up. In the outward rush the door-keepers handled
the small guests rather as if they wrre bags of potatoes
on a wharf, but I suppose boys are used to that sort of
thing and would feel hurt if they didn’t get it. By the
way, when three cheers for (he Queen were called for, a
good portion of the small assembly groaned with gusto.
Lord Jersey gives the newsboys a tea at Government
House on 27th instant. They’ll probably cheer the Queen
there all right enough—and it is to be hoped better
manners will prevail than obtained on last occasion—when
“Ob ! Mr. Jersey, come an’ fire this cove out! ” “ Pass
the butter, please, Mrs. Jersey” were considered by the
young rascals correct forms in which to address their
vice-regal entertainers.
Viewed strictly from a social standpoint, the re-election
of Mr. W. P. Manning as Mayor of Sydney (tor the third
consecutive time) is quite a success. Not that his term
of office has been remarkable for hospitality so profuse as
to be vulgar ; but when he and Mrs. Manning have enter-
tained, they have entertained extremely well. Since
their advent peace has brooded over the Town Hall, and
I am told that the Mayor is the most preoisely-business"
like functionary who has held sway over the civic funds
within the memory of man.
The queer New Australia movement now raging
presents a flowery chance to the woman who gasps for
matrimony and is in a tolerable state of moral and
physical repair. The leaders of the May Flower-ish crowd
who are setting forth from Australia to found a settle-
ment in the Argentine (green fields far off !), “ are ready
to take women members without payment, either with
their husbands—who pay, of course—or as single girls.
One reason for the latter is that a large number of our
members are single men, who desire to marry women of
their own race and language, to make homes for them-
selves, and to lead am honest and moral life. In the
western districts of N.S.W. and Queensland the men vastly
outnumber the women; in Tasmania the proportions
seem reversed ; in the Argentine settlement they want
a fair balance, which is natural in healthy communities,
so that every Jack may have his Jill and every Jill her
Jack.” All the women are to be “healthy and moral,”
and all the men “ strong, straight, and manly,” and
moral, too, and there are to be six of one and half-a-
dozen of the other, and the result will be heavenly—on
paper. Yes, if the new colony lives up to its posters,
Heaven will have a formidable rival in the Argentine
settlement.
Miss Louise Galvin, “ the phenomenal contralto,” made
her appearance as a child of promise in the York street
Hall, on 14th inst., in a concert engineered by Daddy
Hallewell. Miss Galvin is a slip of 16, with dark eyes and
hair as becomes a contralto, and if she doesn’t grow any
more she won’t be too continuous for the tights of Siebe)
and Lazarilloas is her facial double, Madame Yanderveer
Greene. Long legs are as great a handicap to the stage
contralto as a double chin is to the soprano. Little
Galvin’s vocalism is embryotieally sweet, rich, musical
and pathetic, and well merits persistent educating for
the highest rung. Miss Gibson, Mr. Cork and the
Orpheus Society appeared on the bill, where a bald-
headed tenor also disported on the high 0. Humour
insists that this youth is going on the stage, where Ijhope
his scalp will be concealed by a flowing wig. We have
stood the amorous shriek of the tenor with a tar-barrel
outline, and tried hard to believe the soprano really loved
him, but I don’t think we could be carried away by a bald-
headed man warbling on his knees in the limelight.
On dit that a certain ladies’ committee for the Chicago
Exhibition went up the other day to a sculptor’s studio
to view the bust of a legal Sir Somebody ordered for the
big show. The plaster presentment was sloped off (as is
the somewhat top-heavy style of busts) just at the
clavicle, but when the cultured committee females saw it
they were stricken with mortal disappointment. “The
likeness is veiiy good,” said Lady Blank, at last, in a
tearful voice, “but only look at the shoulders I—not half
wide enough. Sir Somebody has fine shoulders—he isn’t
pinched up like this!” “But, madam,” said the
astonished Pj'gmalion, “ the shoulders aren’t depicted
—it is taken off at the collar-bone”—but his remonstrance
was drowned in a war of female voices. “If those aren't
shoulders, / know nothing about art,” said one fussy old
lady, with unconscious truthfulness, and the disgusted
clay-worker (just from Hingland) knew that the Philis-
tines had indeed come upon him !
A certain committee, by the way, has been making an
asa of itself over pictures ordered from an artist to go to
the World’s Fair. First a big one was ordered, and
painted, but when it was up to framing-point, the com-
mittee femininely enough changed its mind, and altered
to a couple of small ones. These were done, and now' the
artist is waiting to hear from that committee, and the
committee has evidently forgotten all about it. The
artist-man has whole stacks of convincing official corres-
pondence, per medium of which he could make these
unbusinesslike seraphs pay up like sportswomen in any
law-court - but he doesn’t want to frighten ’em out of
their wits, and, besides, no man expects anything else
from a woman. But, mind you, there are some of these
daisies who shriek loudest for female suffrage and the
equality of the sexes ! They might pause and reflect that
if the sexes had been made equal, a lot of ’em would now
be telling their ages in the witness-box.
From Messrs. Paling and Co. I have received a copy of
their shilling Xmas Annual with a coloured picture of
Government House by full moonlight on the title-page,
and an assortment of songs within, that ought to meet
everybody’s case, ranging as they do from “ What are
the Wild Waves Saying ”to “ They’re After Me.” The
annual is full music-size ai d clearly printed, and should
appeal strongly to people who like a very wide variety of
ditties.
Yours affectionately,
SATTHO SMITH.

The oldest C.E. clergyman in N.S.W;,
Rev. E. Smith, 8.A., died the other day. He
arrived in 1838 and has enjoyed a pension,of £2OO.
a year since 1863.

The herculean and
once-aggressive Mr. E.
P. Field, the well-known
Sydney barrister who
preaches in the street at
Innoh-tirae and at night,
and has given up his
whole leisure and pro-
bably most of his income,
to the cause of religion,
was not always a pietist.
But in his unregenerate
days, two or three years
ago, he got a nasty throat-
growth, which the local
surgeons told him was
cancer. One need _ not
have read the reminis-
cences of Montagu Wil-
liams, Q.C., to appreciate
the ghastliness of such an
announcement. Mr. Field
at once proceeded to
make peace with his chief
enemy, a well - known
judge, who (characteris-
tically enough) actually
wept over the reconcilia-
tion. Then he proceeded,
to Europe via Melbourne, '
in which latter city Mr.
Iredale, the specialist,
told him that the growth
was not malignant. By
the time he reached Eng-
land the throat - trouble
had disappeared. Mr.
Field, however, regards
his happy recovery as a
special intervention of
Heaven on his behalf,
and has since shaped his
career in a spirit of grati-
tude. The Bulletin
isn’t going to look a gift
horse in the throat, but
cordially recognises the
many kindly deeds which
Mr. Field (and others at
his instance and by the
force of his example) is
doing among his fellow-
cre itures.

the natives in 1821$ and after whom the town was

The other day, iniSydney Supreme Court,
Judge Windeyer told Salomons, Q.'O., to “sit
down, sir !’’ Whereupon Salomons left court
declaring that he would never again practise
before the Judge. However* mutual friends inter-
vening, the trouble was amicably squared by next
day. There has been bad blood between the pair,
dating from the time when Salomons was ap-
pointed to the N.S.W. Chief-Justiceship and
Windeyer said nasty things about him.
“Contrary to expectation, the late Jay
Gould left nothing whatever to charitable or

Blowitz, the Times correspondent, supplies
an instructive advance notice of the Austrian
prince now making his royal way to Australia.
“He has the long, wan face of the Hapsburgs,
sheepish and without character; a leaden eye, a
thin and expressionless mouth, a slow and
uncertain gait. His physiognomy is at once
timid, sly and malicious. . . He is one of the
most ignorant princes of the day. At 18, when
his professors bade him adieu, he burnt all his
books, vowing that he would never touch another
book in all his life, and he has so far kept his
word. . . He has never been able to speak a
word of English.”
There are moments in the lives of each of us
when, in the midst of unexpected, unforeseen danger, we
shall require (o exercise to its utmost extent all the
nervous power we may possess. And there is no greater
foe or danger to happiness than debility, which ought to
he cirefully warded off. In the following case Mr. E.
Robinson, of Tuniul, N.S.W., gives his experience of it:
“For the last three or four years I have been conscious
that my strength, and in an equal degree my spirits, were
waning. I was losing all interest in anything and in
everything. I could not account for it. lat last began
to suspect I might have the seeds of consumption sown in
my system, and that they' were bringing forth fruit. I
saw your advertisement in the Sydney Herald, and
resolved to try what Marshall’s Tonic Nervine could do
for me—this was five months ago. I have completed a
course of 18 bottles, and I can truly say a man in better
health and spirits cannot exist. I eat well, sleep well,
and take any amount of physical exercise, long walks,
and feel no fatigue, and my strength is apparent to me in
every movement I make, every step I tike. All this I
owe to you and yonr sovereign remedy. Let the world
know. You are only too welcome to publish this, in the
hope other poor sufferers may make use of your strength
giving fluid.’’ Will “other poor sufferers’’ lies'tate now?
Marshall’s Tonic Nervine is within the reach of all.

A RACE-TIME REMINISCENCE.
1ST GIRL: “How did you come off over the Cup?"
2ND GIRL: “Went down 10 bob on G’Naroo, but, pooh! I don’t
care. Serves the owner right—they should have jigged"
him for the Caulfield.”
1ST GIRL: “Well, I lost a quid on Malvolio, but you can’t expect
horses to race in a bog, and I’ll be on him again before-
the meeting’s over.”
2ND GIRL: “You let him alone and wait for the Summer Cup.
I know something good, and they’ll have to spiel him
because the stable’s stiff.”
1ST. GIRL: “Rye buck. Ta-ta.”
Parkes, when not whining of His “breach
less home ” and yowling dismally about how he
“now stands in the light of the grave” (sup-
pressedl sobs from his red-headed caricature in
petto, Alfred Allen) sometimes becomes cheeky
and defiant’ and says out loud before the Almighty
that he is going to live until be is 120 years- old!
If so (we have Just been reading up the O’Farrell
business in connection with Parkes’ clumsily-
attempted self-vindication) it vvill take him all bis-
remaining days on earth to explain how it was
that he, when one of' the chief Ministers of’ a pre-
tendedly civilised State, sneaked into a gaol)
planted l a detective-reporter behind a oell-dbor,,
and entering the cell of a helpless man about to
be arraigned for his life at the bar of justice, pro-
ceeded, in > the absence of the wretched prisoner’s
counsel, and without cautioning him as the meanest
policeman would or should l have done,, to extract l
from the miserable lunatic evidence to damn him
at what was practically a drum-head court-martial.
Parkes himself is mad—on the subject of titular
glory and Imperial baubles: The unhappy
O’Farrell had shot a duke of the Blood Royal)
and the soul of the parvenu from Brummagem
went out in horror at the sacrilege. Parkes, who
now can’t have much over 60 more years to live,
professes to believe in God. Let him go down on
his knees for 50 of those years and pray that he
and all the rest of the Treason-Felony Govern-
ment may be forgiven for their horrible blasphemy
against Justice, which, with the equally hideous
Mount Rennie trial, the blood-responsibility of
which rests on the souls—if they have souls—of
still-living men, constitute “ a red-hot hissing
burning brand of shame that will never be
effaced.”
Messi’s. Frost and Shipham, “My Opti-
cians,” of 462. 474 and 587 George-street, Sydney,
issue a lengthy and very complete price-list of
photographic, optical, and electrical goods. No-
body ever realises how many things are embraced'
under these headings until he studies some such
publication as this. The list includes photo-
cameras —about 40 different kinds of them, dry-
plates, lanterns, printing frames, sensitised paper-
chemicals, stands, rectilinear lenses, symmetrical
lenses, iris diaphragms, condensers, slides, view
finders, pocket levels, photometers, aotinographs,.
focussing lenses, ruby lamp, isochromatic plates,.
films, opals, and burnishers. Then the document
gets its second wind and goes off into a great
waste and wilderness of pastes, solutions, varnish-
developers, magic lanterns - several dozen differ-
ent kinds, alum troughs, animalcule troughs,
carriers, regulators, chemical tanks, connectors,.
saturators, generators, lime cylinders, lime
holders, lime tongs, purifiers, retorts, microscope-
attachments, compressed gas, magic lantern slides,
and And after that the book
braces itself up afresh, and the way it goes on
about chromatropes, cyoloidotropes, figureotropes,.
choreutoscopes, pandiscopes, spectacles, eye-
glasses, telescopes, microscopes, barometers, ther-
mometers, hydrometers, barkometers, compasses,,
galvanic batteries, shock coils, magneto-electric
machines, telephones, electric bells, model loco-
motives and steam-boats is something awful to-
contemplate. We now sit down to have a long:
rest.

He came like a Buffalo Bill,.
Ned Kelly and larrikin, cocky,.
Rolled into one, put through the mill;.
And then, sweated down to a jockey:
Jay Gould’s last dealing with his gold
Deserves some admiration;
Unfit for place within the fold,
He would not buy salvation.
’Twixt Jayq when feeling well or ill,
We find no mean disparity—
He lived for greed, and, in his will.
Still scorned the name of charity.

Australia’s present system of Parliamentary
Government, under which the electors have to say
yea or nay to 70 questions asked at once, is as big
a fraud as a physician’s prescription composed of
70 more or less antagonistic ingredients.
It should be no more possible to abolish
payment of members than to abolish habeas
corpus or trial by jury. Payment of members is
as much an integral part of the democratic system
as a man’s heart is an integral part of his body.

All the conservative papers know the
meaning of “Freedom of Contract,” but the
knowledge is confined to the papers. That all
the writers don’t share it is evident from Medway

see the Trades and Labour Council has asked for
a definition of freedom of contract. I have long
tried to find out what is meant by it, and have
not succeeded.” Yet Mr. Day sat in the editorial
chair of the S.A. Register.

The Tories governed the British Empire
for six years on the strength of a majority of less
than 80,000 votes cast in 1886. Now they
prophesy the speedy overthrow of Gladstone, who
had a majority this year of 225,665 votes recorded
in favour of Home Rule.

A few Malays, in North Queensland,
clubbed their miserable earnings to purchase a
boat and start pearl-shelling on their own account.
A petition to the Government was promptly for-
warded by their former employers praying that a
license might be refused to all servile aliens. Yet
we are gravely told in Parliament and the press
that cheap coloured labour does not spell Slavery.

The full secret history of the O’Farrell
case, founded not on hearsay, but on suppressed
and other documents, on letters and marginal
notes and signed statements in the handwriting
of the chief actors of this memorable case—will
ere long be given to the world. It will be written
by the man who of all men is most competent to
write it, and will “ affix the brand of shame where
the brand of shame belongs.”
The Sydney dailies, pleading in chorus to
the charge that they systematically suppress
democratic speeches uttered in the legislature, say
that “they supply the public with what the public
wants to get.” “The worst of lies is the lie
which is half the truth.” The dailies’ plea is
founded on the incontrovertible fact that they
only supply the public with what (by a policy of
suppression and distortion) they have trained the
public to “want to get.”

as a burst of jubilation when he got his title, and
charges the bill to his country. The Bulletin
devoutly trusts that the contents of the hamper
smelt bad when it arrived at the Tower of
London, or wherever Buckley addressed it to.

It is a mystery why the professedly liberal
N.S.W. provincial papers have published without
comment the countless circular letters of the
National Ass extolling the proposed bogus
“ Thrift ” vote. The Bulletin presumes that it
would have been too glaring a swindle— swindling
no objection, only the glare of it—to propose
giving the rich man one vote and the poor man
none, though when it’s boiled down the National
Ass plan amounts to pretty nearly the same
thing.
In the discussion upon the Victorian silver
coinage question, most of the daily papers speak
of the great profit to be made by that province in
the event of its being allowed to coin silver. It
should be remembered that the profit made in
silver coinage is in reality only a loan without
interest, which is repaid as the coining Govern-
ment withdraws the light coin from circulation.
In fact, by buying back the light coin at its face
value, the Government loses on the whole transac-
tion the value of the attrited metal.
Dear Bulletin, —Here is a passage from
Prof. Stanley Jevons’ work “Logic,” a text-book
in the hands of all N.S.W. University students.
Under the head of “Logical Fallacies” he says:—
It is by fallacy of composition that protective duties are
still sometimes upheld. Because any one or any few
trades which enjoy protective duties are benefited thereby,
it is supposed that all trades at once may be benefited
similarly. But this is impossible, because the protection
of one trade, by raising prices, injures another.
If Australia is not a nation of Freetraders, it
deserves to be. Jevons’ is not the only text-book
prescribed by our Universities that is steeped in
this kind of thing.—R.P.T.
The Homestead Settlement of Mylor, S. A.,
held its first annual fete 3rd Dec. in celebration of
the successful starting of a settlement of over 209
‘blockers,” representing families about 809
strong, and to raise funds for a Public Hall and
Institute. There were outdoor sports and an
evening concert. The evidences of the success of
co-operative settlement were to be seen on every
hand, and this is the first real spurt of encourage-
ment these settlements have received. There was
a little overmuch butter and patronage from
Adelaide politicians who came up to see the
settlement, but the Mylorians were too cock-a-
whoop to notice it. A few Mylors in Australia
would put an extinguisher on the Argentina
insanity;
The recent disgraceful scene at Williams-
town (Vic.) Court, when* during the hearing of a
police case, a magistrate, father of a defendant,
took his seat on the bench and strongly pleaded
on his son’s behalf, is not half so shock-
ing as are many other eases in which the
interested advocacy is secret, not open. In remote
country districts the honorary magistrate may at
times be a necessary evil. In town he is quite an
unnecessary evil and should be utterly abolished.
You might as well let a black snake bite through
the carotid artery of the goddess of Justice as
allow people to dispense “justice” to their per-
sonal or class friends or enemies and their brethren
in secret lodges. Petty court cases should be
heard by trained stipendiaries belonging to no
secret society, and Government and the public
should make heavy sacrifices to bring about such
a system.
The Bulletin has again to explain that
the term “National Ass” as applied by it to the
gang which seeks to endow every property-owner
with a double vote for Parliament, is not “short”
for “ National Association.” Ass, in this connec-
tion, really means ass, and nothing else. Ben-
jamin Franklin, in satirising the property-vote
theory, once said that if a man owned an ass (and
a vote on account of the ownership) in the tnorn-
ing, and the ass died at mid-day, in the evening
he would have no vote because he had no ass.
The vote, it was clear therefore, belonged to the
ass and not to the man. The “National Associa-
tions” and “Patriotic Leagues ” and “National
Defence Associations ” and other conspiracies to
defraud the people of their political birthright,
proposes endowing Ass-hood with votes at the
expense of Manhood. That is why the “National
Ass ’ is a name which has come to stay.

The Financial Scandals.
Never, probably, in the world’s history has an
equally young community suffered a commercial
relapse from over speculation and, in some
instances, rank swindling such as Victoria has
lately chronicled. Persons in high places—men
who were generally regarded as models of probity
and stability—are self-convicted jugglers with
other people’s money, who went in daringly for
rash speculations, little heeding that confiding
investors would have to bear the consequences of
probable failure. Permanent Building Societies
seem to have been “ permanent ” only so long as
they were not called upon to meet their obliga-
tions. Banks with high-sounding and compre-
hensive titles, by inordinate advances on insuffi-
cient security, or no security at all, have “shut up
shop ” only when the funds of their shareholders
have been dissipated. The list of widows and
infirm old men and women, of provident people
who had fondly hoped they were secure against
want, but who have been rendered dependent and
penniless by these lamentable departures from
integrity and honesty, is truly heart-rending. It
may be asked what has led to this deplorable state
of things ? Can it be innate wickedness in the
executives of the various institutions at fault?
For the sake of human nature, we will not credit
it. We would rather incline to the opinion that
the ruin has been caused by a baseless sense of
security—by that blind faith in the doctrine of
chance which prompts the almost insolvent
gambler to have one more throw of the dice, one
more hazard of all that is left in the forlorn hope
of retrieving all that had gone. This false sense
of security unfortunately extends to the considera-
tion of more important questions than mere
wealth. The advance of the crisis in one’s life is
unfelt or disregarded—we feel secure in the fact
that a state of good health has been enjoyed—
that trifling pain in the side or back is overlooked.
We have no precedent for personal disease, and
therefore it is impossible to create one. Rash
reasoning this ! —this over-speculation on the
health of the past taken as guarantee for the well-
being of the future—yet how common it is. Now
we give a truth so trite as to have become almost
an axiom. Kidney trouble is the primary occasion
of the greater proportion of known diseases. The
symptoms are most insidious in their nature—
often imperceptible in relation to the organs men-
tioned, but generally manifested in other parts of
the body in the shape of headache, neuralgia,
rheumatism, urinary complaints, sleeplessness,
&c., &c. H. H. Warner & Co.’s Safe Cure is the
one disinterested liquidator of apparently hope-
lessly insolvent constitutions. It is an investment
that pays more than 20s. in the <£, and is likely
to continue to do so until the advent of the last
man.

Society
A man who boarded an outgoing ocean
steamer at Circular Quay, Sydney, the other day,
just as the captain was farewelling his numerous
friends, noticed that the gallant mariner, a very
handsome fello\V indeed, not having time to be
more formal or to explain matters, enthusiastically
kissed every woman (and there Were over twenty
of them) who came to say good-bye. They couldn’t
have been his sisters, or sisters-in-law, or anything
of the kind, for they were all more or less good-
looking, and were of the most diverse shapes and
complexions. When a man has so tremendous
an amount of love to distribute at high-pressure
over so wide a surface, it is impossible for him to,
in each case, enter into detailed explanation.
Kiss ’em till you raise a blister and hug ’em hard;
you can do this in 30 sec., and it saves hours of
palaver.
The amusements at Melbourne Govern-
ment House on ball-nights are rigorously restricted
to indoor pastimes; flirtations in the spacious
grounds being sternly discouraged. There is but
one open door, and no pass-out checks are issued.
In Gov. Loch’s time couples used to wander where-
soever they pleased, and lose themselves on the
Yarra’s bank for whole evenings at a stretch.
But we have changed all that now. Hopey, per-
haps, has more experience in the ways of men
and maids, or, again, the W.C.T.U. may beat the
bottom of it; but probably the escapades of those
frisky high-class dames caused this' step to be
taken.

A very well-known Australian medico is
caught on bird-lime. He married a pretty Aus-
tralienne some time ago. Now his first wife, mar-
ried in England, threatens to move legally, failing
plenty of backsheesh.

infant.”
• # *
The time-expired Kanakas are settling
down and marrying in Moreton Bay. They easily
obtain work at 15s. per week, evicting the white
men wholesale. Half-caste children are gradually
becoming as plentiful as blackberries. Many go
to the kirk regularly and affect to be beastly
religious. #
Bartlett, M.P., who was summoned before
Adelaide Police Court on December 9 for the
maintenance of an illegitimate child, was defended
by Kingston, Q.C. He escaped the snare laid for
him and left the place “ without a stain on his
character.” He even had costs awarded him.
The awkward baby was born six years ago, but
action was not taken till last month, and then it
was initiated by the Destitute Board, and not by
the mother, who was in Mr. Bartlett’s employ for
a fortnight in December, 1886, but had not seen
him either before or since. “We shall none of us
be safe soon !” said Kingston, Q. 0., with well-
simulated horror.
* # *
She was a solitary teacher in a lonely
N.S.W. bush school, and the inspector called to
hold the usual school examination. When school
was dismissed, he turned his attention to the
teacher, and examined her quite closely. Then,
evidently satisfied with the inspection, he drew
her to his fatherly bosom, kissed her tenderly,
cocked his hat on his left ear, sighed deeply, and,
asking the way to the nearest pub., vanished.
The pretty teacher is wondering if all this is part
of the official routine.

Speaker Bent, of Victoria, as a councillor
of Brighton, “heartily endorses” a proposal to
introduce surf-bathing off the local beach, men
and women to bathe together. It takes an awful
lot to induce some men to wash themselves.

The Imperial Hotel, Blue Mountains
(N.S.W.), burnt down the other day, was a
hostelry with a history. It was built by the
original seed land-boomer of modern days, Father
Deeming, who spent all his own and his sister’s
money in wild-cat schemes, and then made himself
scarce*

Many habitues of Sydney Town Hall
festivities regret the re-election of Mayor Manning.
When a return ball was given to the Mayor those
who had enjoyed his hospitality were invited to
join in. Numbers of them refused, and their
names have been carefully handed to Mr, Manning
by one of the return-ball promoters.

the Melbourne Shakespeare Society, demonstrated
that Christianity is responsible for the existing
prejudice against red hair. Worshippers of Thor
venerated crushed-strawbetry-hued hair. Chris-
tianity overthrew this small deity, and brought all
his institutions into contempt. We object to red-
headed girls on religious principles.

# # *
In Melbourne Supreme Court, in a very
fishy case, the other day, a beautiful lady dumb-
founded Dr. Madden, Q.C., by assuming too
much knowledge. She was describing the passage
in a bijou cottage where the learned counsel had
never been. She called him “Doctor,” and told
him how a certain room opened on to the corridor.
“You know, Doctor,” she explained petulantly.
“Indeed, madam, I do not; I have never had the
honour ! ” And then everybody laughed, and the
anecdote was trotted out about old Aarons meeting
a very ladylike person in a railway-carriage and
calling at her house at her special invitation to see
the chrysanthemums, and then the next day the
alleged husband called and borrowed <£6 from
him, and all was forgiven.

Government officials are prohibited from
receiving (without permission) purses of sovereigns
and testimonials when being transferred. But
the bridge is got over by the handing of the
souvenir to Mrs. G-uv’menter. _ Lately, the pro-
moter of a N.Q. testimonial cautiously interviewed
the lady—“ ’Ow will yer take it, marm? Tools?
Plate? Halbums ! Or sovs.?” “I prefer it in
sovereigns, thank you, as my husband can accept
a present from me.” And so it fell out. Which
reminds us of a case, somewhat 'opposite. The
lady wanted jewellery. “ But yer carn’t hev
joolery.” “Why not?” “ ’Ow’s yer ’usband a-
going to pay his lickcr score ter me if you don’t
take it in sovereigns? ’’ “ My husband’s personal
debts are not my concern.” “ ’Ow would yer like
me to summin him for refreshments supplied, an’
beds, an’ meals, an’ osses an’ traps, an’ ’e sup-
posed ter be chummy alius at ’ome with you ?
The lady “took it in sovs.” —or, rather, agreed to
accept it in sovs., and feelingly responded on be-
half of an empty purse.

The surrender of ex-bank-manager Ben-
nett, who got away from Paddington, Sydney*
three years ago, leaving behind a £IO,OOO to
£IB,OOO shortage, and who, though believed to
haVe cleverly escaped from Australia, has all the
time been located in a Sydney suburb, incites The
Bulletin tp several remarks. Firstly, that when
your “smart” detective has no letters from a
man’s Wife or girl to “steam” in the post-office
he is as helpless as a legless man without crutches.
Secondly, when an absconder dyes his skull-thatch
red he completely baffles the world, which contains
no one (not even a detective) base enough to
believe that any man could be so ruffianly as to
voluntarily endow himself with ginger hair.
Thirdly, that Bennett—who hadn’t a shilling
when he gave himself up—is, after all, a had
business man. Knowing that there was a £SOO
reward for his arrest he should have got a friend
to deliver him up to justice (the justice which
gave Abigail five years and let off arch-fiend
Nathan with nine months) to claim the blood-
money for the benefit of the devoted wife who has
kept her husband’s secret while the world was
being raked in search of him.

Down .South.
The snigger at Cairns, N.Q., is against a
Majah and 16 mashahs who dolled and perfumed
themselves to go out to the expected 8.1. steamer,
because they had heard that six lovely girls
were aboard that boat, en route from Britain to
Australian “ lady-helpdom.” Several hours of
tossing about in a small steamer and —only five
chawbacon farm-labourers as a reward ! Nausea.
# * *
That is a gruesome story (the subject of a
magisterial inquiry) of a Brisbane hospital warder
and a dying patient. Another patient talks of
having seen two punches in the pit of the stomach
bring on a speedy death-rattle ; the other side s
storv is that the old man with the fractured thigh
fought fiercely and was simply made to lie down.
Anyhow, fancy even a slight scuffle between a
strong, healthy warder and a dying man ! Ugh !
* # *
Fox, a Victorian painter, has just ended
his six years’ Parisian study, and returned to
Melbourne, with a number of his works, which
rank him with Longstaff, Altson, Streeton, and
the others who have done so well. Here is another
reminder of the extraordinary neglect of French
art by the authorities of Melbourne Gallery,
although every one of their pupils goes for study
to Paris, the head-quarters of art.
* * •
Victorian artists shook their feathers and
looked proud the other day at the announcement
that John Mather had been appointed a trustee
of the local National Gallery. This was followed
by the cable news that little J. Forde Patterson
was working up an exhibition of Australian
nictures in London.

Victoria needs a divorce Judge all the
year round. Suitors are furious over delay.
“HIS DEATH ECLIPSED THE GAIETY OF
NATIONS."
Thej’ come and go, these jesters gay,
Their lives are short and merry ;
I?hey have their fling and fade away;
Old Charon, grim, and gaunt, and gray
Rows Leslie o’er the ferry*
A tear drops in the flowing howl,
Our solemn toast is “ Rest his soul 1"
Speak kindly of the fallen star.
Whose art was ever jolly*
We mind the time he “ bought the bar ”
And lightly puffed from big cigar
A cloud of fleeting folly.
The other day the World awoke
To find him vanished, like th'e smoke*
He might have filled a foremost place
In true dramatic story ;
He might have gone a slower pace,
And lasted longer in the race
For evanescent glory.
But there—how many graves are green
With raem’ries of what might have been !
And many lives of brighter men
Than he have been more barren--
For thousands, counted by the ten,
Recall the melting moments when
They laughed at Fred, and Farren.
He earned the kindly epitaph—
“ A man who raised a real laugh.”
Heigho ! how long since Nell and Fred,
Disported here so madly ?
A year or two—and one is dead,
And one, they say, lies sick a-bed,
Her day is closing sadly.
What prophet ventured to foretell
This gloomy end of Fred, and Nell?
The game goes on, and people new
Are blowing burlesque bubbles
(Though smaller than the others blew)
And watching them provides for you
Distraction from your troubles,
But hands and feet would fain encore
The pair who will appear no more.
GONE AHEAD!
The bard he paces Melbourne streets,
His locks are long and lank ;
In musing tone he oft repeats
Those words of tailor Frank—
Of Frank, whose heart for Matthew T).
Exceedingly hath bled;
And still the bard declines to see
Where things have gone ahead.
The bard he smells the city fair
And sniffs financial stink ;
He notes fat, loafing persons there
Whose hearts are black as ink,
Who make a composition while
Their betters want for bread,
The devil take the windbags vile
Who blew the land ahead !
The bard has heard of money “ made,”
Or stolen (all the same),
And freely spent; ’twas “good for trade,”
And brought the robbers tame.
The champagne days are vanished now,
Like phantoms have they fled,
And taxes tell the toilers how
The country “went ahead.”
A curse upon the buildings tall
With scores of rooms To Let,
The buildings tall whose owners all
Live well on “ paper ” debt.
A curse on “ farthings in the pound,"
Of which you’ve lately read ;
There’s rot throughout the land renowned
For having “ gone ahead.”
They echo in the bardling’s ear,
Those words of tailor Frank,
But still he cannot drop a tear
For rogues who break a bank.
The bard he toddles home, and when
He dozes off in bed
He dreams of choking many men
Who sent the land “ ahead.”

* * #
Victor Houghton, who shot himself the
other day in Sydney, after losing all his own and his
wife’s money, some .£13,000, had long made up his
mind to suicide. He repeatedly told a friend that
bed never be dependent on charity, and that
when his last shilling had gone he’d blow out
his brains. Heaps of people talk like this: few have
courage enough when the time comes. Houghton
was a queer card. He called himself “ Sherbrooke
Houghton. “That,” he used to openly say,
isn’t my name, but it sounds well, so I use it.
JNobody can stop me.”

One of the small
industries- of Sydney
is the sale of mourn-
ing-cards. The man
who runs the trade is
an ex-publican with a,
talent for doggerel, a,
red nose, a sad coun-
tenance, and a seedy-
black suit surmounted
by a heavily-craped
bell topper. When
he hears of a death
he promptly fixes up
eight or ten lines of
verse suitable to the
case of the deceased,
and copies it out on
a black-edged card.
Then he puts on the
black suit and the
midnight hat, and
inserting his lugubri-
ous countenance in
the home of woe he
offers the document
for anf-a-crown. And
his aspect is so
horribly in keeping
with, the melancholy
occasion that it seems
like profanation to-
kick him out, so he
gets his- two-and-six
regularly.

The agitation for a foundling-hospital is
again struggling to the front in Melbourne. It
was started originally some years ago by that
“ sterling ” philanthropist, Charles Raymond
Staples, now under committal for entrapping lone
widows and others by “ Anglo-Australian ”
balance-sheets.
* * #
The lodger again. A man was charged,
the other day, at Melbourne, with threatening to
chop down ” with an axe a person who boarded
in his house. The host and his wife had taken in
this individual to eke out their humble income,
and he went the way the lodger always goes and
took to hugging his landlady when her husband
was out. Then the alleged master of the house
tried to eject him. “I’ve told him hundreds of
times to leave my house,” said the defendant
sadly, “I’ve been cool to him, I’ve sworn at him,
and I’ve fought him, but still he won’t go.” And
so far as The Bulletin knows, He hasn’t gone yet.
Ihe man who takes in a lodge! generally finds,
sooner or later, that it would have been cheaper
to let his spare room to a big centipede.

■ Hobart is afflicted with a teetotal Mayor
who insists upon the toast of Her Gracious
Majesty the Queen being drunk in cold water.
Ihis is the deadliest blow yet aimed at
Imperialism by any Australian official. Royalty
and whisky suits many, but Royalty and water !

Bassingthwaighte is the oddest named
Melbourne shopkeeper.
The Colonnade Hotel, situate on Brick-
field Hill, Sydney, is one of the very few first-class
hotels in the centre of the city. It is a large and
elegant structure in the busiest part of George-
street with splendid dining, drawing and reading
rooms, comfortable bedrooms, and high-class
table. _ Also, the charges are of the lowest, and
the fluid refreshments ate beyond reproach.

When the eyes become weak or the lids inflamed
and sore, a scrofulous condition of the blood is
indicated. For scrofula, in all its forms, Ayer’s
Sarsaparilla is the best remedy. It invigorates
the blood and expels all humors.
Dear Bulletin,— There were evidently
two vessels named the Lady Franklin. In old
Hobart cemetery I found this inscription
“Sacred to the memory of John Butterfield,
native of Bradford in Yorks, who lost his life by
the total wreck of the Lady Franklin on his pas-
sage to Port Phillip, April 21, 1838, aged 31.
When the tempest was raging and thou wert
distrest, I trust the Lord sent His angel and bore
thee on to the blest.” Perhaps this is the ship
which Marcus Oiarke alluded to. —Yours, W;

At Hughenden (N.Q.) was seen the other
day a team of 25 billy-goats drawing a cookie’s
load of two tons.
The S.A. Government has a jhuraorous
Way of trying a blackfellow for murder. He is
hauled before a magistrate, charged, and evidence
given. Then he is asked what he has to say.
Nigger (real N.T. case): “Me no killem first
time; boy killem first time; him close up dead |
me killem little bit.” A confession sufficient to
Imng even a Matt. Davies ! Then the beak com
mits the nig. for trial. At the superior court the
Crown mercifully appoints a lawyer to defend
him ! —after he has owned up in full and with
that owning appearing on the court records!
Why not appoint a lawyer to defend him before
his untutored mind is cheated out of a confession
that makes the lawyer a useless fee-snaveller ? Or
else let the beak take him outside at once and
brain him. “ Much less Christian ?” Much more
merciful 1
Minister R. E. O’Connor’s reply to a depu-
tation from Newcastle, asking the commutation
of the sentences of the Broken Hill strike-leaders
was type-written. Which recalls the fact that
when the historical Mount Rennie deputation
waited on Gov. Carington, the latter immediately
read out his answer from a MS. prepared before-
hand. “ But, your Excellency,” ejaculated Dailey
to the Baron, “ that has no reference whatever to
the arguments we have advanced.” Carington
angrily put up his hand, with “ I do not wish to
be interrupted.” “I despise such a man; not
only has he no heart—he has no brain, which is a
greater crime on such an occasion,” said Dailey
subsequently to the present writer.
Trickett, who in N.S.W. nominee Legis-
lative Council moves the emasculation of the Elec-
toral Bill by the double-enfranchisement of soul-
less Property, was formerly a member of the
popularly-elected Assembly. Would he, if still a
Member of Assembly, dare to publicly declare
in favour of balancing a man’s life against an
allotment? Never! Where is the Member of
Assembly, out of the whole 141, who dare stand
up and make such a declaration ? There is not
one. The N.S.W. House of Kidney-fits says the
One-man-one-vote question was not decided by
the electors at last general election. A fact: Only
one solitary candidate, out of all who stood for
election, dared to oppose the bedrock democratic
principle. His name was Burns, and he was re-
jected.
The Newcastle (N.S.W.) wharf labourers,
now on strike, relied to a certain extent upon the
co-operation of the steamers’ engineers. As cloth-
ing specially designed to resist the extreme cold
is never worn by the mutton-stowers, a few extra
turns of the refrigerating machinery would have
driven the hardiest blackleg to the upper deck.
Instructions were, however, issued to the engineers
not to reduce the temperature below a certain
minimum.
A CLINCHER.
QUEENSLAND LIAR : “Why, you N.S.W. chaps don’t know what heat
is. You want to go to Queensland to feel heat.”
NEW SOUTH WALES LIAR : “Well, I dunno. Some places down here
I’ve seen it 130 in the shade for days and nights at a stretch.”
QUEENSLAND LIAR: “Hundred and thirty in the shade! Why, up
on the station, we’ve had it 150 in the water-bag at four
o’clock in the morning!”

The brig Velocity, one of the last of the
old Tasmanian whalers, was destroyed with gum
cotton the other day. The ship was built in
England in 1807 and for long was engaged in the
West African and West Indian trades —carrying,
it is said, many a load of—well, they call them
“blackbirds” in Queensland—for the Cuban
planters.
The present Kaiser, ever since his acces-
sion, has been steadily weeding out the old veteran
generals of the German army. The process is
now about complete. The great military machine
is run by a crowd of gaudy young officers, most of
whom never saw war ; and when the next trouble
arises they and the Kaiser may be very like the
tame bear that tries to drive a locomotive, and
can’t stop it again.
Cowley, Minister for Lands (Q.), has
resurrected one of MTlwraith’s old swindlecates.
Mac. wept over the ruins of Rockhampton, and
urges them to save their city by agitating for a
land-grant railway thence to Port Alma at the
mouth of the Eitzroy. If constructed, the
swindlecate will own the deep-water terminus of
the central line, a 350-mile State-railway. The
channels of the Pitzroy are being allowed to silt
up, for economy ' sake, you know. The swindle-
cate hopes to eat at a gulp 350 miles of rails
sleepers, carnages, locomotives, stations, &c.’
built at the expense of the confiding taxpayer
and fix its own price for the feed.
THE GRAVE IN A HEART.
(FOR THE BULLETIN).
He came to love with a foul love,
When I loved her with a fair,
lie conquered ; and then 1 killed them.
They are buried together there.
And sometimes I sit in my garden and think,
On soft, sweet summer morns,
“ Ah ! dear God ! may we not pluck one rose
But we mangle our hands with thorns.”
D.W.

TTORTICULTURAL society of new
X 1 SOUTH WALES.
A SPECIAL GENERAL MEETING of the
above Society having been duly called under Rule
1G of the Constitution, NOTICE IS HEREBY
GIVEN that such meeting will be held at the
Y.M.C.A. Hall, corner of Pitt and Bathurst
Streets, on THURSDAY, stb of JANUARY,
1893, at 7 p.m.
The business, as set forth in such requisition, is
as follows :
FIRST —The propriety of members of the
Council acting in the capacity of Judges
and Stewards;
SECOND —That it is expedient that the
Auditors furnish a Half-yearly Report;
THIRD —As to the advisability of amending
the Constitution by the appointment of
two governing bodies, viz., a Council,
consisting of a President and twelve
Vice-Presidents, and a Committee, also
of twelve, to carry out the detail work
of the Society. The work of such Com-
mittee to be subject to the revision and
supervision of such Council.
H. RAWES WHITTELL,
Secretary.

Answers to Correspondents .
Vo notice will be taken of communications which arrive
at this office insufficiently stamped. Name and
