“ George Mackay ” (Maoriland): We adore
originality, so here are your verses :
“ THU FAREWELL SMOKE BEFORE WE GO.
“ Thiet of the modern Providence—the guinea Omni-
potent—
Tallow-clothed plutocrat, with the long, cruel, tentacled
paw,
And the yawning abyss-paunch and the bottomless pit of
a maw,
Though the world and its crushed soul are in the shadow
of your claw,
In the roaring social furnace hearts are heated white with
wrath,
Which Time’s blows shall beat and shape to countless
swords to lay you low.
Blind Justice won’t be drugged for ever: sit still and
think of this
When you puff the weed-reek from the last cigar before
you go.
“Able-bodied fellow-toilers, able-minded men and true,
Nature’s filial sons and daughters, helping Nature in the
fight,
In the world-wide social battle with the darkness and the
night,
For the cause of Truth and Justice and the triumph of
the Right,
Ere the silent sleep be on us, ere we hear Time swish his
scythe;
Ere our souls pack up and bundle, w'hen the pulse beats
faint and slow,
Our pet pipe we’ll fill and to the cause the fragrant
incense blow—
When we sit and dream over the farewell pipe before we
go.
“We are the crew of the old Earth-ship and steer her
through the storm,
And tho’ our captains all are dead, ironed, murdered,
crucified,
Their charts ‘faked,’ we’ll reach some port beyond the
waters deep and wide.
Their restless spirits grasp our keel and the wreckers are
defied,
And when that port’s in sight our whole life in one big
quid we’ll chew,
And its dark, strong juice and weed upon th’ Eternal Sea
bestow.
Spray and coal may corn and grime our soul —that sea
will wash us clean !
Our last thoughts, what price? over the farewell quid
before we go !’*

“ Hail to each fair moonbeam.” That’s enough. “ Hail,”
like “ Wot ho,” is the interjection of a barren mind.

meet again so oft before ” is the worst mixed sentiment
we ever met with “Victorian”: Try to find anew
synonym for “the prevailing depression.” It is an
aggravating phrase.... “Oswald Hugo”: The man who
is haunted by a face seen in a previous state of existence
is cheap, stereotyped mysticism. Dreams of that sort
are selling now at seven for 6d. .. “New Australia”:
Perhaps you are not aware that the banks of a river are
not necessarily a well-watered region. You can fall from
the centre of one of the most awful deserts on earth rio-ht
intoagreat river 1200 miles long “ P.J.E.” (Number

den”: We have done Griffith’s epitaph long ago
“E.A.F.”: N 0.... “Northerner”: If you sent £1 to the
quack in question and he failed to forward the medicine

had arrived you would be dead by now “L. Moore ”

we wouldn’t have your stilted “youth of noble mien” at
a gift —“Wascoe”: “White Petticoats” was stolen
by the paper in question, from the Hawk, without

Nature. It cleanses the pores, leaves the skin free for
impure matter to exude. Is it harmful ? You may as
well say, Is it injurious to wash your scalp and remove
the dandruff? for removing the outer cuticle is of the
same nature. ■
It is not necessary to be confined to the house. Use
moderately, and you will suffer no inconvenience. Tell
your husband, your physician ; explain to them it is a tonic, a medicine—not a cosmetic, not a powder;
that it is not to enamel, but to cure you; that it is perfectly harmless, free from arsenic, lead,

own face is proof; that she uses it on her own daughter. Would she do this if hurtful? Then, ladies

Aboriginal Samuels, onoe champion runner, is
now living in a Sydney suburban mia-mia with his
wretched gin. Also, his wretched rum—when he can
get it.
» # *
A wood-chopping match for .£lOO and the
inevitable championship will be a feature of the Christmas
Festival at Melbourne Exhibition. Weary fathers who
have to rise with the lark and slice fuel for the winter Are
are expected to come in their thousands and learn what
Miserable Objects they (the weary parents) make of them-
selves When they wallop their box blocks with a blunt
axe. The axemen entered for this Melbourne contest cut
wood as though it were bacon, and make a point of
shaving themselves with their trusty steel before laying
into the lumps of timber.
# * #
Adelaide cricketers reckon Ferris the best
bonder who ever visited Adelaide. On the billiard-table
wickets Turner, Spoft'orth, Palmer, Lohmann, Attewell,
and others, were harmless, but Ferris secured a big
average against S.A. two years ago. Giffen places Ferris
first in the world’s bowlers.
# * *
George Giffen, the S.A. champion, has played
jn senior cricket since he was 16 years old. Giffen
gtarted off with six duck-eggs, and would have been left
out of the Norwood had it not been for the veteran
“ Jimmy ” Gooden, who still plays with the club, Giffen
is 84 years old.
# # #
Adelaide cricketers were greatly enraged over
the team selected against N.S. Wales. They expected to
see at least three of the crowd out of it and juniors
selected. Blinman and W. Gillen on form ought not to
have been picked.
* * *
Pierce was sent to Adelaide with the N.S.W.
team specially to put Lyons out. His “sugar-doodles”
had the desired effect, and he howled Lyons just when
the crowd thought the giant was settling down to knock
one clean out of the universe. Sir John Moore’s funeral
was a perfect babel compared to the silence of the
Adelaidians as Lyons marched back to the pavilion.
* * *
The V.R.O. committee, by a narrow majority,
agrees on members voting by post, a measure to restore
the waning influence of the Athenseum Club—O’Shanassy,
Purves, & Co. Alleesame Sydney Union Club and the
A.J.C.
* # *
Goddard has challenged Slavin, and the latter
has accepted. The champion rushers at last meet. The
irresistible encounters the immovable. It reminds one of
the man who bought two locomotives, put them under
full steam, and started them in opposite directions on
the same line of rail.
* * »
Champion oarsman Stanbury is at present
engaged in erecting telegraph-poles on the Taralga-road,
near Qoulburn, N.S.W. Of what is this a sign—the
decadence of sculling as a “ game,” or the improvement
of scullers as exponents of honest and steady industry ?
# # *
At Sydney California Club, last Saturday
night, two men met (in the presence of the police) to
bang and disfigure each other for a small amount of
money, and Alexander Stewart, the worst-banged man of
the pair, died. Arrests for manslaughter have been
made, and the moral press looks with a grieved and sur-
prised expression upon the incident, and says that it is a
Wrong Thing. The grief may be natural enough, but the
surprise is certainly quite uncalled for. When two men
meet to smash each other “to a finish”—which simply
means till one of them is half, or more than half, defunct
—it is quite probable that the loser may become, by mis-
adventure, a little more defunct than was intended, and
may stay that way. The principal cause for surprise is
that such an accident doesn’t happen more frequently.
Also, the legal aspect of the case is hard to understand.
The police are present at these savage assaults, and never
interfere unless one individual seems to have half his
swollen head in the grave, therefore they are either
accessories to the crime, or else it isn’t a crime at all—
up to the moment when they interfere. In the present
case the row stopped the moment _ the police
intervened, but Stewart died from injuries which
he had previously received under the authority
and supervision of the force. Therefore The Bul-
letin wants to know why the police haven’t been
arrested as well as the manager of the establishment and
six or eight other onlookers. Or, if the police were acting
under instructions from the Government, then why hasn’t
the Government been arrested.
# * *
One prize-fighter—or a dozen prize-fighters for
that matter—isn’t much loss to the community. And his
death presents one bright feature which he never pre-
sented when he was alive—it will probably put an end to
professional pugilism locally. The mangling of one’s
fellow-man for a few pounds in cash is a degrading busi-
ness, as well as an outrage on the picturesque and the
beautiful. The average bruiser isn’t lovely When
whole and unsmashed ; when freshly licked, and
painfully removing his bloated head to the
nearest hiding-place, he is a blot on the landscape
and a sin against all the rules of perspective. The Romans
did things better in the days of the Oatsars ; they had the
fallen gladiator killed and dragged away with a hook
before his ugliness had time to soak into the public
comprehension. #
The latest cablegrams concerning “Wells, the
Englishman ” (who at one time was apparently in league
with the Anglo-Australian cableman), are not calculated
to lure punters to Monte Carlo. The former bank-breaker,
in gaol on a charge of colossal fraud, has attempted to
dash out his brains against the wall of his dungeon.

Mick O’Brien, best-hearted of jockeys, provided
for his father in his will, and left £5O .or £lOO to each of
his brothers and sisters. The young widow takes some
£4OOO of personalty and a reversionary interest in a free-
hold property, and poor Mick’s share of Tradition falls to
Phil. Heywood, who has now got the unlucky animal all
to himself.

Rowdy Lord Ailesbury recently cwushed the
secretary of the stand-offish Ist Zingari (London) Cricket
Club. Ailesbury drove up to the Club ground with the
precious colours of that institution attached to his hat,
and the secretary was told off to enquire by what right
the outcast nobleman assumed the emblem of a club he
didn’t belong to. Then came the cwusher. The secretary
was reminded that a former Marquis of Ailesbury had
been humbly requested to allow the Ist Zingari to adopt
his family colours, a favour which he “ graciously
granted.” There’s a moral to be found in this yarn.

Mushroom racecourses spring up around Mel-
bourne at the rate of two a month ; there are now 18
within a 15-mile radius. South Yarra pony track, the
latest addition, which combines Chinese tugs-of-war and
balloon ascents with the refining influence of pony-racing,
is six laps to the mile round.

team to Adelaide because he is saving himself and his
holidays for the English trip.
“IT IS THREE THOUSAND MILES
LONG.”
We allude to the great Mississippi River in
America. For hundreds of miles of its course it
is yellow with the earth which the swift current
has torn from its banks. Probably the Missis-
sippi bears constantly in solution ground enough
to make many a rich farm, though the amount is,
of course, greater at times of flood. Thus, for
drinking, and even for washing, the water of the
Mississippi must first be purified.
It is the same with anything which either for
appearance or utility is in some way thrown out
of character. For instance, the blood in our body
is a river. From the heart to the extremities it is
of a bright red colour, but on its return through
the veins it is dark and sluggish. The reason is
that when it starts from.the heart it has just been
purified, and when it comes back it is loaded with
all sorts of refuse matter it has picked up in its
journey. In other words, the blood—which is
only food fully digested—carries life on its red
current and brings away disease and death on its
dark one.
Speaking of her own condition at a period about
six years since, a woman writes that her skin
gradually became first yellow and then of a saffron
colour. Connected with this she experienced
several symptoms of illness. She had more or less
pain at the sides and considerable difficulty in
breathing. Her appetite, previously good, began
to fail, and after eating comparatively little, there
was much distress in the region of the stomach.
There was an attendant loss of energy, both of
mind and body, and she felt tired, languid, and
weak.
Now we must observe that in ease of natural
fatigue from labour or any exertion rest is an
unfailing remedy. Sleep and relaxation renew
our powers. But there is a kind of fatigue which
rest does not relieve—for which the Angel of
Sleep bears no healing on his wings. What vile
counterfeit is it ?
She adds : “ I finally took to my bed and was
visited by a doctor, who said my liver was wrong,
and that I had jaundice. He gave me medicine,
but as it had no good effect he advised me to go
to the hospital. As I objected to this, he said I
might try a change of air and see what that would
do for me. So I gave up my place and went to
my home at Fairford, in Gloucestershire. Fail-
ing to obtain any benefit from the change, I con-
sulted another physician, who attended me for
some time, but I grew no better. Whatever I did
I could not recover my strength, and my friends
thought I was in a decline.
“I did not eat enough to feed a bird, and began
to despair of ever recovering my health. With
many ups and downs in the way of feeling, and of
fear and hope, I lingered along until July, 1890,
when an acquaintance recommended me to try
Mother Seigel’s Syrup. Indeed, so much was I
impressed with what was said about this medicine
that I sent my niece four miles to procure a bottle
of it. After taking the contents I felt better. A
weight seemed to be lifted from my chest, my
relish for food returned, and by degrees I found
myself in every way stronger. I will conclude by
saying that after having used two bottles more of
the Syrup I was able to return to Birmingham
quite well, and have had no signs of the ailment
since. It is only just to mention that I feel better
now than I have done for many years, and had I
sooner known of Mother Seigel’s Syrup I should
have avoided an amount of suffering and misery
which it saddens me even to look back upon.”
The lady referred to is Miss Sarah Hawkes, of
the “Lion Inn,” Longmore-street, Birmingham.
That yellow or saffron colour of the blood was
caused by the failure of the liver to secrete, or
take away, the bile from the blood. Remaining
in the blood it was carried to all parts of the body,
and necessarily showed itself through the skin in
the ghastly hue she names.
Understand, please, that the bile, useful to help
move the bowels, is a poison when in the blood.
The entire malady of biliousness in all its forms
is a symptom of indigestion and dyspepsia, the
source of most of our aches, pains, and diseases.
It is no new thing to say that the success of
Mother Seigel’s Syrup in reviving the torpid
digestion, stimulating the liver, and thus purify-
ing the blood, sets it apart from all other medicines
as an article to be relied upon when the use of
ordinary drugs has been followed only by disap-
pointment. The blood holds the secret of life,
and this remedy keeps that ruby river clear and
clean.
Historian : “ Madame Roland was the
intimate enemy of Marie Antoinette. ”
A memorial service was held in a London
church on the anniversary of the death of W, H.
Smith. The world needed a reminder that the
successful book-stall proprietor had lived and died.
The moment a child is born, that moment
he begins to die. Beware of disease making in-
roads into the system. Probably one of the
earliest signs is slight dyspepsia which cannot be
attributed to errors in diet, want of, fresh air, &c.
It is a nervous kind of atonic dyspepsia, some-
times combined with acidity from liver sympathy.
There is not the same relish for food, and so relief
from this state is sought for in piquant relishes,
wine, or tea. Another symptom is an uneasy or
tired feeling about the head—fulness, it may be,
or sleeplessness, and stupidity, especially after
eating. The brow is often hot, so is the top of
the head, and the hand is pressed wearily across
the eyes. Life begins to lose its brightness ; then
things begin to go a trifle wrong, and there are
moments of peevishness and irritability. Low-
ness of spirits is succeeded by want of sleep.
There is danger ahead, if not, indeed, close at
hand, and happy is he who sees the signal in time.
Recourse should immediately be had to some
genuine tonic restorative which will restore the
consumed nervous tissue, and most probably
Clements Tonic is the best for this purpose, it
having had uniformly good results every time we
have seen it used. In oases of debility, nervous
prostration, bilious and nervous headaches,
anaemia, and all diseases arising from poorness
of blood and indigestion, Clements Tonic can be
used with confidence. It is not a quack remedy,
and, unlike other patent medicines, its proprietors
do not claim that it “will cure anything,” from
lock-jaw to bad spelling. But for cases of affec-
tions of the nervous and digestive systems it is
specially serviceable. No doubt of the merits of
the article can exist after a perusal of the evidence
of John Tippet, Esq., Glenrowan, Victoria, who
writeslt is a very pleasing duty for me to
convey to you the facts of the great benefit
that has accrued to me through taking Clements
Tonic. For the last two years I have suffered
very severely from indigestion, flatulence, and
I think every other horror appertaining there-
to, being subject to uneasiness and retching after
meals, especially after dinner, which meal I had
to discontinue altogether, the nasty sensation and
retching depressed me so much. I tried many of
the advertised cures without benefit, but on read-
ing of a similar case to my own cured by Clements
Tonic, I resolved to give the same remedy a trial,
and after taking three bottles the uneasy feeling
and retching were entirely cured, and I shall not
fail to make it known as widely as possible in the
Glenrowan district, where I reside, and trust it
may be the means of relieving others so afflicted.
On rising every morning my mouth would be stuck
together. I was advised to try' ■. I did so,
bub only got temporary relief. I had made up
my mind to go to Melbourne to see if I could be
cured. Being advised to try Clements Tonic, I
did, and now, thank God, I am twice the man.
Night after night I have lain in my bed and never
shut my eyes, and would be more tired when I
got up than when I went to bed. The bad taste
has left my mouth and I feel quite a different
man, thanks to Clements Tonic. —I am, sir, yours
respectfully, John Tippet, Glenrowan, Victoria.
It is the ambition of the great Mutual
Store, situate in Flinders-street, Melbourne, to
embody every trade and industry under the sun,
and to supply every possible want' of a civilised
community. It has got very near to the realisa-
tion of this ambition. The big building opposite
the railway station now contains a grocery, wine
and spirit store, drapery, tailoring, boot and shoe,
and mercery departments, wood and coal depot,
furniture, china, glass, carpet, ironmongery, sta-
tionery, and fancy goods emporiums, grain and
produce store, chemist’s shop, livery and bait
stables, and department for of surgical
appliances. When a savings bank has been added
it will be difficult to imagine what more the heart
of man can yearn after —unless it be a “tote.”
Dear Bulletin,— Re “A. J. Miller,” supposed
to be Brtlce, alias Wallace. If Miller is the Bruce, alias
Wallace, then I am only one of his many victims in this
district; but my relations with him were purely business.
Melbourne Sporting Standard, Deo. 1, ’9l, . I
him as being perfectly straight. On the faith of this I
took £1 7s. 6d. worth of advts. from him for insertion in
the press, but he “cleared" without paying a cent. A
detective-sergeant tells me one Wallace worked a similar
dodge in Brisbane in 1891, and that the victims of his
bogus sweep wrecked his office. Wallace is a short, stout,
fair man, hook-nosed, with florid complexion, no whiskers
and long moustache. — T. P. Hughes, Albury.

He Bought a Horse.
“My dear,” began Mr. Cityman, as he entered
his suburban home the other evening in apparently
high spirits, “ I did a good stroke of business to-
day.” “Indeed! And what was that?” archly
inquired Mrs. C., with a well-meant though un-
successful attempt to appear interested. “Noth-
ing short of a dead bargain. I arn going to utilise
the old stable where the fowls are housed, and I
mean to ride into town every day. It will benefit
my health, and I won’t have to hang around the
streets waiting for a ’bus.” “So you have
bought a horse, then?” was the acid rejoinder.
“Of course I have; you don’t suppose I’d buy a
donkey, or a mule, or a dromedary, or a locomo-
tive, or ” “ Well,” broke in Mrs. C., in con-
ciliatory tones, “I didn’t know that you could
manage a horse, and besides I should have thought
” “Oh, certainly,” snapped out Mr. City-
man, “ you’re always thinking instead of making
sure. But when you see me coming in after
a rattling good canter you’ll change your tune.
I tell you this horse is a beauty, and no mistake,
quiet as a child, no vice about him, good pacer,
and warranted not to shy or cut up capers.”
Early the following morning the steed was
brought home and handed over, and the proud
owner, after several desperate tries, finally
succeeded in climbing into the saddle and set off
for town. There was no wind to speak of that day,
and a small boy on the wayside was frantically
endeavouring to persuade a recalcitrant kite into
a recognition that it was destined for a nobler
career than scraping up road metal, when a spry
horse and a perspiring, doubled-up equestrian
hove in sight. There was a busy, exciting time for
about three minutes during which the air was
filled with profanity and buttons and things, and
then a riderless horse might be seen ambling
peacefully down the road as if nothing had
happened. Mr. Cityman took the next ’bus home,
where he humbly submitted himself for repairs.
These his wife soon effected with the aid of a
valuable family remedy always kept in stock by
her. The next day a horse was advertised “for
sale, cheap,” and Mr. C., while distinctly dis-
claiming all desire to “make an advertisement of
himself,” felt bound to admit that his wife was
right again when she preached the doctrine that
St. Jacobs Oil, for the absolute relief and cure of
contusions, sprains, and bruises, knows no rival,
All chemists and storekeepers sell it.

RABBIT EXTIRPATION
FELTON, GRIMWADE, & Go’s
Patent Phosphorised Wheat.
As the season for vigorous action for the ex-
tirpation of the Rabbit Pest is now approaching,
the manufacturers desire to call the attention of
station-owners and managers to the latest ex-
pression of opinion they have received as to the
efficacy of their
PATENT PHOSPHORISED WHEAT
in the
DESTRUCTION of RABBITS.
“To Messrs. Felton, Grimwade, and Co.,
Melbourne.
Dear Sirs, —
“ I am laying the Patent Phosphorised
Wiieat now, and have better grass than
any in the district, still the rabbits will
take it in preference to the grass.
“I cannot speak too highly of the wheat,
and have used it this last three seasons.
Last year I did more with four bushels
than I did by the expenditure of =£2oo in
carbon bisulph, and digging out. To
quote a neighbour’s opinion, it would be
cheap at a pound a bushel. I have given
samples to a number of persons in this
district, who will use it this coming
season.
“To conclude, there is only one way to
deal with the rabbit plague—by poison ;
all the exterminators and quack patents in
the colony, among which may be included
carbon bisulph., are not worth the tins or
cases they are forwarded in. The reason
is obvious—the time required and cost.
“The use of your wheat should be made
compulsory by Government, and the
rabbits would vanish.
“ Yours truly,
(Signed)
“14/11/92.”
“E. C. PETTCH,
“ Warra Warra,
“ Via Stawell.

The Village Commune.
AN AUSTRALIAN LABOUR POEM, BY ALARIO.
It is the sin of sins
That one must ask another’s leave to toil,
And Labour, oft must starve when wanting work.
It is the wrong of wrongs that any man
Should seek to profit from another’s toil,
And Living Labour, made in God’s own image, be
Considered but a CHATTEL to be bought and
sold—
To-day be hired and to-morrow cast aside.
Not less a bitter wrong it is, that he
Who owns the instruments creating wealth
Can starve his brother into bondage,
But thinly veiled when masked with Freedom’s
name.
For Capital, more strong than Governments,
Holds arbitrary power of life and death,
And for a mere caprice, a trivial fault,
Or to entrench itself ’gainst glut, or loss,
May close the factory, shut down the mine,
And bar the means of work against the serfs
Whose toil created them, and who—if right
Were done—would own them still.
And thus the chattel slave of olden time
Become the wages-serf of modern days.*
Though He, who never rests, but ever steers
The stately caravels, whoso silent keels
Plough heaven’s blue immensity, decreed
The saoredness of Labour, made the earth
A workshop which our toil must sanctify,
This sacred Living Labour is esteemed of far
Less worth than Capital, a thing inanimate.
Humanity, which ever lives and learnsf
And carries in its bosom all the hope
Of progress for our race in future years,
Is valued less than minted gold ;
And men before the Golden Calf of Capital
Bow down like serfs, like serfs adore.
Whene’er depression comes, Humanity
Must first be starved, or turned adrift,
Before the “ sacred rights of Capital ”
To interest and profits are denied.
’Tis strange the “ sacred rights of Labour ” are
So seldom talked about—the right to earn
A sustenance for wife and child, to build
A cottage home and live a human life.
For God, we’re told, so loved accumulated wealth
That He created all the vast expanse
Of this revolving globe for Capital alone,
And then created man, its humble serf,
To minister unto its luxuries. +
Upon the anvil of Despair, bedewed
With sweat and tears of toiling multitudes,
Must Labour forge the mighty dollar for its use.
And swell its hoards while toiling millions starve.
Thus Capital neglects its sacred aim,
To be the heart of Industry and pump
The life-giving corpuscles of warm blood
Through all its veins ; to foster, not exploit.
But, like the octopus, it spreads abroad
Its suckers, seeking whom they may devour.
And Interest is an anti-social force
Which rends the golden bonds of brotherhood —
That sacred brotherhood of man Christ preached—
And crushes Labour to support in luxury
The few who never toil, nor spin.
Grim Architect of Poverty, it gives
To wealth created in the time of Noah
Immortal life and power in perpetuity
To suck the veins of Living Labour ;
Nor ever shares the loss arising from
Vicissitudes of seasons, drought and flood.
In plenteous years,
When Nature whispers to the bursting seed
Awake ! put forth each tender leaf and shoot
And robe the earth in loveliness ;
In cloth of gold where waves the rip’ning corn,
In mingled green and gold where zephyrs sport
Among the tasselled maize; and richest green
Where over hill and dale the pasture spreads
Its rolling billows; deck its breast with siiips
Where daisy-craft unfurl their painted sails of pink
And white and gaily ride at anchorage,
Moored side by side with clover blooms that toss
And heave whene’er the fitful summer winds
Bend low the supple masts;—then and then
Alone may Capital claim interest
In fair proportion as its uses tend
To raise the harvest. Most unjust it is
It Capital demand its pound of flesh
In years of iron skies, or devastating floods,
When all the earth is one vast wilderness.
In seasons such as these must Labour bow
His humble head to Capital for bread,
And one by one the smiling homesteads fall
Into grim Shyleck’s greedy maw, and then
The independent yoemen cease to be.
And ev’n the mind of man
That, like the eagle, seeks to soar and scan
The whole circumference of this round earth
And all the worlds beyond—e’en that
Is shackled. Who may articulate the wrongs
Of his dumb, suffering brother in words that burn
Like living coals, made incandescent with
The sense of grievous wrong, but Capital,
Wide-minded Capital, will boycott him
And call him “ agitator,’’ worse than thief,
And sentence him to starve while tantalised
By looking at the wealth he may not earn ?
But still each one must fight as Nelson fought,
His blind eye on Expediency, his eagle gaze
On that far-distant signal-post in heaven
Where Duty’s signal flies. Nor cease till Death’s
Cold fingers run the shot-torn ensign down
And waft the hero-craft into those silent ports
Where, after turmoil, there is peace.
, What tongue can voice
The children’s wrongs when Capital kidnaps
Them from their play, and forces them to toil
And sweat in grimy factories before
Their lives, like tender buds, can well have spread
Their opening petals to the breeze?
The Arab slaver well may smile and deem
His crime but as a sin most fashionable
Whed “ Christian gentlemen ” can interweave
In spools of yarn the threads of children’s lives.
The thread is long, for God upon the reel of life
Spun years a full three score and ten,
But factory shuttles flay the bobbin bare
In life’s young spring, in reckless greed for gold.
And then a little starveling finds a grave
Among the many nameless graves which mark
The paupers’ only resting-place on earth.
Ah ! once I dreamed
That Capital, in lieu of human serfs,
Essayed to harness eagles to its oar.
The harness was of finest silk, embroidered o’er
With precious stones, and decked with waving
plumes.
But they, the free-born wanderers of the sky,
Repelled with scorn the gilded chains and scoffed
At wages-slavery, “Harness the winds,” they
cried,
“That whip the ocean surges into foam,
Bridle the mighty coursers of the deep
That lash their snow-white crests against the
crags;
And cage the hurricane; but leave the eagle free.”
Yet one of them, ensnared by cunning wiles,
Was harnessed to the car. In vain it lashed its
wings
And tore the galling chains with beak and claws.
Then realising that it was indeed enslaved,
It drove its beak into its mighty heart
And tossed its life-blood to tliemigbty winds, and
cried :
“ Bear witness, ye, before the mountain crags,
And hymn it to the rocks that I chose death
Itself to slavery.” And ever since
The traveller in the rocky pass may hear
The storm wind sing—
The fetters of the slave '
Must by himself be wrought;
Heroic men and brave,
Heroes of deed and thought,
Prefer a freeman’s grave,
And on the dead men’s plains
They spurn the slaver's chains.
Ah me ! If men were men with lion hearts
And eyes that glow witli Resolution’s fire,
Like sunken coals, undimmed, unquenchable,
Oh ! what a band of Labour Knights would ride
Abroad to rend Oppression’s chains and hurl
That tyrant, Capital, from its despotic throne.
Then would the hearts of men be temples meet
For Liberty to dwell, nor be defiled.
Behind each brow would glow the noble light
Of God-like consciousness, and crown the face
With manly beauty, pow’r, and will.

labourer, or the workers in the match-factories, with that

Here at Last 2
Some seven years ago a strange thing happened to the lady
whose photo, appeals herewith, whereby she became
A MYSTKRY
Which no one can explain or account for so far, not even her
husband. How it happened was this : She lay dying of a compli-
cation of diseases ; often she would faint off, and lie unconscious
as long as 30 hours at a stretch—frigid as a corpse and apparently
dead. The leading medical men of Melbourne were in attendance,
often at a cost of Five Guinean a day, yet she sank lower and
lower. Everything that medical science could suggest was tried ,
even morphia failed to give enough ease for two hours sleep
daily. At last, in desperation, some said, “ Try Mesmerism-.
Mr. Harle, as a Mesmerist,
Had been very successful on the public platform, and had also
saved life by private magnetic treatment; but Mrs. Harle, being
a lady of very pronounced opinions and strong mind of her own,
never could see anything in it but a hobby of his, and so, when
urged to try it, she ridiculed the very idea ; but they said, ‘ Ivy
it for sleep only— you will go mad for want of rest.” For a long
time she could not bring her mind to submit; at last, after much
prayer to God for guidance and help, she w'as willing to try.
After about half an hour’s mesmeric influence and passes, she
went off into a peaceful hypnotic state, which lasted, as was
intended, from eight at night till ten next morning; and she
awoke rested and refreshed. The experiment was repeated, when
Lo! Everyone was Startled
■
Mrs. J. N. HARLE.
To find that, whilst to all intents and purposes she was asleep, yet in another sense, the mind was far more awake,
and she commenced talking about what the servant was doing on the ground floor, what
mile awav what was happening in the home of a dear friend some few blocks away, and so forth. Then she vyas
requested’to look into her own body ; this she did, and was surprised herself, and surprised everyone else by tracm,
the cause of her trouble, which proved to be mainly an internal cancer. She then told them how to cure hei.
Crowds of Friends Came to See Her,
For she had a large and influential circle of acquaintances. She astounded all by her marvellous powers of vision-
to see into their bodies as if they were made of transparent glass, to see the members of their families, to know all
about their commercial affairs, seemed quite easy to her ; England, America, New Zealand, etc., seemed just like next
door ' The Sensation was Immense!
Drawing-room, Sitting-room, Dining-room and Bedroom were crowded with inquisitive people, and the invalid became
a phenomenTn’. The doctors were nonplussed with their patient-she gave directions how to so ten.thecancer to the
lancing state: this was done ; then, as they dare not give chloroform on acamnt of her heart, Mi . Ha her under
mesmerism, whilst Drs. Teague, of Collins Street, lanced it. She awoke without having felt anything that had hap
pened. Everyone said: “ Start and use your powers for doing good, you can
Save Lives by Thousands.”
But her health was too low. First, she cured herself—in three months’ time she was again taking her accustomed
drives on the Esplanade, in her phaeton, to the astonishment of the whole district. To properly tone the system a
change of climate was needed, and she came to N.S. Wales. For a short time she resided in Maitland ; her retreat
was known by those who were aware of her powers, and she got no peace till she agreed to start practice. Five years
ago Mrs Harle opened in Newcastle ; three years out of the five have been spent travelling in the Northern district,
and away in the bush discovering gold mines, and she seems to be able to find gold as easy as tiace disease.
Seven Thousand People Have Consulted Her
In her mesmeric state; some about business, but most about their health. Not one can say: “I am worse for your
treatment,” whilst all who have followed the advice and treatment, rejoice in restored health. Mrs. Harle describes
each diseased organ, finds the cause of every trouble, and prescribes remedies and treatment. Her wonderful mental
vision or clear sight enables her to see just what medicine, and how long it will take to cure it, often giving the
medicine three months ahead, and never changing a bottle.

If vou wish to mortally offend Mrs. Harle, just class her along with the card-reading, palm-scanning, fortune-telling
crowd and the task is done. No such rubbish 1 Mrs. Harle is mesmerised by her husband, and when in the hypnotic
state she sees with her mind. She cannot open her eyes, yet she hears, talks, asks and answers questions, etc. When
the mesmerism is undone, she awakes, and knows no more of w'hat has happened, than if she had been chloroformed

A Bushman’s Song.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I’m travelling down the Castlereagh, and I’m a
station-hand,
I’m handy with the ropin’ pole, I’m handy with
the brand,
And I can ride a rowdy colt, or swing the axe all
day,
But there’s no demand for a station-hand along
the Castlereagh.
So it’s shift, boys, shift, for there isn’t the slightest
doubt
That we’ve got to make a shift to the stations
further out,
With the pack-horse running after, for he follows
like a dog,
We must strike across the country at the old jig-
jog.
This old black horse I’m riding—if you’ll notice
what’s his brand,
He wears the crooked R, you see—none better in
the land.
He takes a lot of beating, and the other day we
tried,
For a bit of a joke, with a racing bloke, for twenty
pounds a-side.
It was shift, boys, shift, for there wasn’t the
slightest doubt
That I had to make him shift, for the money was
nearly out;
But he cantered home a winner, with the othe r
one at the flog—
He’s a red-hot sort to pick up with his old jig-jog-
I asked a cove for shearing once along the Mar-
thaguy:
“We shear non-union, here,” says he, “ I call it
scab,” says I.
I looked along the shearing floor before I turned
to go—
There were eight or ten dashed Chinamen
a-shearin’ in a row.
It was shift, boys, shift, for there wasn’t the
slightest doubt
It was time to make a shift with the leprosy about.
So I saddled up my horses and I whistled to my
dog,
And I left his scabby station at the old jig-jog.
I went to Illawarra where my brother’s got a
farm,
He has to ask his landlord’s leave before he lifts
his arm ;
The landlord owns the country side—man, woman
dog, and cat,
They haven’t the cheek to dare to speak without
they touch their hat.
It was shift, boys, shift, for there wasn’t the
slightest doubt
Their little landlord god and I would soon have
fallen out;
Was I to touch my hat to him ? —was I his bloom-
ing dog?
So I makes for up the country at the old jig-jog.
But it’s time that I was movin’, I’ve a mighty
way to go
Till I drink artesian water from a thousand feet
below;
Till I meet the overlanders witli the cattle coming
down,
And I’ll work a while till I make a pile, then
have a spree in town.
So, it’s shift, boys, shift, for there isn’t the
slightest doubt
We’ve got to make a shift to the stations further
out;
The pack-horse runs behind ns, for he follows like
a dog,
And we cross a lot of country at the old jig-jog.
B.
Waiting for the Leader.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
O when will our-great leader come—
Our weary hearts begin lo fail,
And foes on ev’ry side assail,
In hordes we cannot sum?
We need one who will bravely bear
The heat and burden of the day,
Who will not faint upon the way
O’er-vvearied with despair.
One who will not through traitor wiles
Allow his honour to be sold ;
Who’ll scorn alike the tempter’s gold,
A ruler’s nods and smiles —
Who’ll bear a high and stainless name,
And choose the stern and virtuous part
t That bigots may not break his heart
Xnd hound him down to shame.
Who’ll lead the people into light,
And cast the greedy tyrant down,
And spoil him of his bloody crown,
And turn the wrong to right.
The millions scourged by Mammon’s whips
Are biting savagely their chains ;
Their masters tremble for their gains
And whiten to the lips.
We’re arming for the fight that comes
Sure as day follows on the dawn,
And loudly on some dreadful morn
Will beat the battle drums.
Law’s tedious course is all too slow—
Law helps the rich to spoil the poor—
The keen and flashing sword is sure
To heal the wrong we know.
They prate to us of lawful means ;
“ Make laws for your redress,” we’re told—
They buy our leaders with their gold
And laugh behind the scenes.
For centuries of bitter wrong
Have made it almost seem as right;
And all the harder is the fight
That custom’s power is strong.
But now the wide world is awake—
And, seething like a lava flood,
The fire is mounting through the blood
That soon its bounds must break.
And when the accepted leader comes
’Tis then the crimson flags will fly,
And the war cries break against the sky
With the beating of the drums.
And as the bursting thunderstorm
Will clear the foul, unhealthy air,
And leave it with a fragrance rare,
And earth with brighter form,
So with the pent-up righteous wrath,
That bursts its human bounds at last,
And seems to devastate and blast
But purifies the earth.
L.

The Reformer.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A world that is drenched with a rain of tears
That shut out the light of the sun,
An endless eddy of hopes and fears,
Whirling ever through endless years,
Ah, when will their course be run ?
Then out of the darkness gleams a light,
Fair, hopeful phrases fall,
And hearts are moved for a bitter fight
That will set all things eternally right 5
Then the end—a funeral-pall.
’Twas only a flash of light in the dark,
And the noble effort was vain,
And the weary life, like a dying spark,
Vanished at last, leaving never a mark,
And the world has its same old pain.
Clermont, Sept. 26.
F. E. P. McDONALD.
A very old stager, just returned from the
North-West Pacific, called in at The Bulletin
office a few days ago and gave us the particulars
of a tragedy which, although it happened away
back in the “Fifties,” is full of interest in con-
nection with the escape of convicts from Australia
and Norfolk Island, and which, so far as we
know, has not been alluded to by any writer. The
incidents of this act of piracy were related to him
by two of the actors therein. They were at
that time (1875) both old men and on their way
from the Caroline Islands to the Pelew Group
with their wives and families, when they—with
the man who tells their story—were cast away on
one of the Caroline Group and lived together for
15 months. One was a white man named Harry,
and the other a native of Ocean Island —where the
tragedy occurred—named “Ocean Island Jack.”
In or about the year 1845, Harry, with four-
teen or fifteen other escaped convicts from
Australia and Norfolk Island, arrived at Plea-
sant Island in a whaler. This whaler had
worked her way through the various groups to the
Kingsraill Group, and it was at Drummond’s
Island (Taputeuea) that Harry and two others—
named Wilkins and Brady—came on board, and
asked for a passage. To their surprise they found
five others on the ship, picked up by the whaler at
some other island; one of these was a man
formerly known to Harry on the Hunter River as
“Cow-face,” who had, on two other occasions,
escaped and been re-captured and heavily flogged.
The captain of the whaler (the Amity K.) landed
the lot on Pleasant Island. Shortly after they
were joined by seven other escapes, and they
attached themselves to the six or seven different
native clans of Pleasant Island, and lived the
usual wild life prevalent in those days. In two or
three years four of the lot had met with deaths
from violence, either by the natives or from
the hands of their comrades. Harry and some
five others, among whom were Wilkins, Brady,
Macdonald and “Cow-face” (the latter’s surname
was never known) then went tb liVe oh Ocean
Island, distant aboht a day’s sail away—Ocean
Island being in lat. 0.50 South, and long. 168.20
East; and Pleasant Island lat. 0.28 South, and
167.15 East —both almost on the Equator, and
about 3000 miles from Sydney., Here they led the
same sort of life As at Pleasant Island. One day in
December, 1852, the whaling brig Inga, of New
Bedford, IJ.S.A., called at Ocean Island, and in
the evening the convicts formed a plot to cut her
off, which was successfully carried out by the aid of
the Ocean Islanders, led by “Oceanlsland Jack.”
The captain and a boat-steerer and two others
went with Harry to get some pumpkins,
growing a few hundred yards from his house.
Harry complained of being ill and went back
fot a drink, leaving the captain with Jack,
Who, having enticed him out of sight of the
three others, stabbed him to death while drinks
ing from a cocoanut. The boat-steerer and the
other two men, and also ten men on liberty, were
all murdered, but so qiiietly Was it done that on
the ship, which was lying-to within a short
distance, not a sound was heard to alarm the
mate. Three of the convicts were on board, and
were having dinner with the mate, a man named
Nash, and other officers, when the brig was rushed
by a horde of natives, many of them boarding in
canoes, others swimming off. In a few minutes
every man of the crew was killed except the
cooper, who was in charge of the deck, and who,
after killing a native with a whale-spade, took
refuge in the main-top, where he soon died from
loss of blood and fell to the deck. The ship was
then looted and set on fire, but fearing the
smoke and flame would perhaps bring up another
whaler, she was hurriedly scuttled, and sunk irt
59 or 60 fathoms of water. For about a couple
of years after this the Ocean Islanders (at
the instance of the whites) would not hold any
communication with ships for fear of discovery of
the end of the Inga. Wilson (who killed the
second mate and steward with a horse-pistol)
went away to Valparaiso in a whaler, and died
there. Brady and “ Cow-face ” were both killed
on the island. Of the fate of most of the others
on Pleasant Island nothing is known. Harry, up
to 1882, was living on one of the Pelew Islands,
in good circumstances, and Ocean Island Jack
died on Ngatik, near Ponape (Caroline Islands),
only a few months ago. Pleasant Island is still
as thickly populated as it was in the “ fifties,” but
things are changed there now, it having been by
some curious arrangement annexed into the “Mar-
shall Island Protectorate ”by Germany. There is
a Deputy-Commissioner there, and no English or
other foreign vessel is permitted to call and trade
locally without first getting a clearance from the
Commissioner at Jaluit, in the Marshall Islands.
As for Ocean Island, its lot has been a hard one,
successive droughts having ruined and decimated
its once teeming population. The few survivors
(about 300) have barely enough cocoanuts to live
on, supplemented by pumpkins. They do a good
trade, however, in shark-skins and fins with Ger-
man trading vessels. The droves of sharks at
Ocean Island are a sight worth seeing.

THIS MAGNIFICENT ESTATE of 239 ACRES has been
* surveyed by Messrs. Miller and Lewis, of Pitt Street,
Sydney, into 138 Allotments, ranging from 1 Acre
Residential Blocks to 10J Acres, situate only 500 yards
from the WENTWORTH FALLS RAILWAY STATION,
on the opposite side of the line. It embraces all that fine
and elevated land opposite the mansion of R. M. Pitt,
Esq., whose splendid garden excites the admiration of
every passenger on the Great Western railway.
When the delightful climate of Wentworth Falls is taken
into consideration, the place may be safely described as
The Madeira «f (he Blue Mountains.
Delicate constitutions and worn-out business men are
benefited equally by the refreshing purity of the 'mountain
air, and the estate is not surpassed for residential purposes.
The FONTAINEBLEAU ESTATE
is well described by the poet, viz.;
The waratah, all gleaming red,
Lifting in pride its stately head,
Shines through the clustering ferns ;
While as the wattle’s yellow bloom
Falls in soft showers, its deep perfume
The air to sweetness turns.
Would you this haunt of beauty know?
’Tis the charming Estate of Fontainebleau !
The Blaxland Road is now being made from the Railway
fence, and, as;it runs right through this magnificent estate,
it will, when finished, be without exception the finest
Carriage Drive on the Mountains.
The FONTAINEBLEAU ESTATE is the most elevated
and beautiful about Wentworth Falls far and away
superior to the land on the other side of the line. The
picturesque views and the extreme fertility of the soil
renders the place very attractive, and specially adapted
for Fruit or Flower Gardens, Orchards or Dairy Farms,
for which Nature seems to have intended it.
